Vol 16. — -No. 781. j
[Saturday, February 2, 1895.
Registered at the G-eneral Post Office, Sydney, for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper,


THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
By Reef and Palm, Louis Becke’s fine set
of South Sea tales, is to hand in fresh
quantity (paper, Is 9d ; cloth, 2s 4d), and
orders addressed to The Bulletin office
will be promptly filled. All admirers of
Becke’s talent will be glad to hear that
his book continues to sell brilliantly, and
is so far the success of Unwin’s “ Autonym
Library.”
The Play Actress, by S. R. Crockett (paper,
Is 9d), is the fourth volume in that series,
and is pretty thin and weary. Crockett is
neither a Barrie nor a Stevenson, and since
“ The Raiders ” he has written nothing in
any waf r memorable.
The New Party, by various authors (sloth,
2s lOd), is a cheaper reprint of the big
book in which the Isocrats, or equal-rulers,
some time ago announced their advent.
So far, the New Party seems to have fallen
rather flat ; and the book in which they
bring the socialistic creed up to date con-
tains rather too much emotional rhetoric,
and waves too many fine ideas too wildly,
to be of much intellectual profit.
Harry Custance’s Riding Recollections (cloth,
3s) is also a reprint of a bigger book ; and
sporting men will find the reminiscences
of the well-known jockey very readable
indeed.
The Parasite, by Conan Doyle (paper, Is 3d),
is a short, readable, ephemeral tale, with
a moral pointed against wicked hypnotists
and indiscreet hypnotees.

Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The anchor’s
best book, well printed.

Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is.

( Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.

sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.

Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.

3s. A charming English romance.

Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.

Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or

Cloth, 4s. A readable tale about Australia.

Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.

6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.

fages. Cloth, 5s 6d.

Cloth, 6s. 6d, Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief among‘ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
Noughts and Crosses, by A. T. Quiller Couch.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Some of the, quaintest,
sweetest stories ever penned, with art
reaching its acme in deliciously subtle
simplicity.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Pest-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The

A really fine novel, epic in breadth and

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
Life's Paradoxes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Strange ! The man who works the hardest never
makes a pile of pelf.
And the flirt who flirts most madly sometimes
falls in love herself ;
Strange 1 The bogus banks have buildings in the
very finest style,
And the simpler the Celestial looks the deeper is
his guile;
And the wight who wades in wickedness feels not
the direst woes,
And the man who drinks the deepest doesn’t have
the reddest nose.
Strange! that when the man who owes us leaves,
we’re all with anguish torn,
Bub when the man who pays departs there’s not a
soul to mourn ;
Strange ! The rooster on the steeple, though he’s
only made of tin,
Is more famous than the live one with his merry
morning din ;
And the fool who boasts a title (like the steeple-
cock on high)
Is looked up to and respected when a sage is hustled
by.
Strange 1 The man who stoops the lowest nearly
always rises high,
And the parson says we only live to learn the way
to die;
Strange 1 We hail the badge of slav’ry as the
banner of the free,
And we nail the thieves and prophets side by side
upon the tree ;
Strange ! The heathen throng the thickest where
the clergy tithe the sod,
And thousand temples rise to Man for one that’s
built to God.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Farmers and sailors are not the only
persona who anxiously scan Wragge’s forecast.
A well-known Sydney dentist says that so long as
there is moisture in the atmosphere he can rely on
a stream of patients.
Editor Bulletin, —Two drunken swaggies
were holding a theological discussion outside my
bedroom window last week. Grand finale : “You
blanky Protestant,” said Pat, “ you will never go
to Heaven as long as you live !” And then they
fought, and I rolled over and slept.—O.E.H.,
Bathurst.
In a SA. witness-box. Plaintiff sued
defendant for cost of making tank. Bench to
defendant’s witness: “What do you think of
tlje job?” “Well, I think it’s a shplendid job,
a foine job; but, begorra, it’s a rough job, a
very bad job.” “Does the tank leak?” “ Yis,
it lakes about a fut, or 18 inches, or two fut, or it
moight be a yarrud. ’ For plaintiff: “Do you
know how much a yard is?” “Is it double or
single width ye mane?” “ You can step down.”
“ Thank yer Honour.”
Dear Bulletin, —Is the kangaroo a hideous
animal? Mrs. Henry Wood (a bad novelist, but
the best-read of the day) writes thus in “Lord
Oakburn’s Daughters," p. 355 : “If she were as
hideous as a kangaroo.” I always considered the
kangaroo and horse the most graceful animals I
have seen, especially the red kangaroo. They are
certainly a long way before either the aborigine or
Chinaman, both in point of good looks and clean-
liness. And have you ever seen the kangaroo pro-
perly drawn ? In nearly every advt. his tail is
wrong, be., doubled round towards the point. Now,
a kangaroo always wears his tail straight out
behind. The socub-wallaby, however, gets his
between his legs when camped. I claim that
kangaroos are bipeds.same as Mrs. Henry Wood
herself, and, like her, make soup out of their tales.—
Gun yah Tom.
“Outis,” of Sydney Telegraph, lately
complained, with the voice of one personally in-
jured, that Premier Rosebery is going the way of
all upstarts. When he was a small politician,
just visible on the horizon, he was profoundly
deferential to the press—he was deucedly civil to
it, by the way, when in Sydney, and used to stay
up talking to scribes till four in the morning—but
since his elevation to the premiership he has as-
sumed a lofty attitude and looks on the Archi-
medean lever as so much offal. “ It>is human
nature, no doubt, that beggars thus straddled on
swift horses should ride rapidly devilwards.”
Now Rosebery, as it happens, isn’t known to have
shown any special signs of inflation, and even if
he had, there is no visible reason for the D. TVs
agonised wail on the subject. In reality it is
N.S.W. Premier Reid and not Rosebery that the
Telegraph is wailing about. Reid was a tame
politician in days gone by, and used to feed out of
the hand, and Telegraph reporters used to bail
him up in the street and demand information,
which was always given cheerfully. But now
Reid, who used to rush round to several newspaper
offices and write leading articles, doesn’t feed
out of the hand any longer, and he tells the reporter,
per medium of his office flunkey, to call next cen-
tury, and the Telegraph curses Rosebery instead
of him, because it doesn’t want to show that it is
hurt and yet it must curse somebody. In which
respect it is very like the cross individual who
comes home and kicks the dog because it would
damage his Christian character if he kicked his
wife.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— R. A. Thompson and Co., S Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 2, 1895.
Practical Federation.
The appointment of Mr. Eddy to be Chief
Commissioner of New South Wales rail-
roads for another seven years is one of the
most fortunate things which has happened
to that province since Parkes’ retirement
from politics. The Sew South Wales rail-
ways represent £36,000,000 of borrowed
money, and the management or mismanage-
ment of this stupendous asset is therefore a
matter of the first importance. It is pos-
sible, as experience has shown, to so elabo-
rately misconduct the lines as to make them
combine bad accommodation, heavy charges,
and a very serious annual loss which has to
be made good out of the public revenue. It
is also possible to so conduct them that the
charges may be reduced, the accommodation
immensely improved, and the lines made
nearly, if not quite, self-supporting. Until
the advent of Commissioner Eddy there had
never been, either in New South Wales,
Victoria, Queensland, Tasmania, or Maori-
land, a railway - manager who adapted
himself thoroughly to the conditions of
the country. Some, like Speight, being
themselves of English origin, insisted
on constructing the whole railway system
on the English pattern, regardless of
the fact that a country where the average
population equals five persons to four square
miles cannot run its lines on the same prin-
ciples as one that supports 200 or 300 times
as many people on a similar area. Others
had been brought up among local conditions,
but lacked technical training ; and between
the two classes the history of Australian
railroad control was mostly a story of
muddle, drift and hapless experiment. Until
the advent of Eddy, the State railways of
Australia were, in some respects, almost as
ghastly a financial failure as the private rail-
ways of the United States and many other
countries, and the community owes him a
debt of gratitude for proving that State con-
trol can be more efficient, more honest and
more profitable than is private enterprise,
and that, too, under immeasurably worse
conditions.
« « •
How much worse these conditions are is a
matter which is seldom taken into account.
In the first place, Australia has a larger
extent of railway in proportion to population
than any other land in the world. The only
countries which anything like approach it in
this respect are Canada and the United
States, and even they are some distance
behind. Moreover, many of the lines in
the United States and almost all of those
in Canada were assisted by large Govern-
ment subsidies in money or land, or both ;
and the private companies which own them
have only to earn interest on their own
capital and debentures, which often repre-
sent little or no more than half the actual
cost of construction. In Australia, on the
other hand, the railways are expected to
earn interest on their entire cost, which
places them at a serious disadvantage when
comparisons are instituted between them
and the American “ roads.” Moreover, the
Australian lines are laid out, to a great
extent, by political amateurs, and laid out
badly as a consequence ; they were built
with about the most expensive labor on earth;
the materials employed were imported at
great cost from the furthest end of the globe,
most of them at a time when freights were
very much higher than they are at present;
and the capital required was borrowed at
prices ranging from four to eight per cent.
All these charges incidental on construction
in a new country were debited to the rail-
ways, which started work handicapped with
a heavy load of indebtedness and many other
disadvantages.
• # #
Then, for many years, the system of man-
agement was even a shade worse than that
of construction. To put it at its worst, it is
only needful to say that it was almost as bad
as that which prevails on most of the United
States railroads—a condemnation which is
sufficiently sweeping for all purposes. Under
thoroughly efficient control, it is quite possi-
ble to bring things to such a point of perfec-
tion that a respectable mediocre person may
'thereafter keep them in excellent working
order merely by maintaining the policy
which he inherited from his predecessor; but
until the advent of Commissioner Eddy none
of the Australian railway systems even had a
chance to attain such a condition. Their
management was equal to the average, but
the conditions of the country made it
essential that it should be much better than
the average if satisfactory results were to be
attained.
• • •
Meanwhile, the fact that New South Wales
has secured for a lengthened term the
services of the only thoroughly successful
expert in Australasia suggests an opportunity
of taking the first practical step towards
that much-talked-of Federation which, so
far, has consistently refused to come any
nearer. The Victorian railways are just
now in a worse condition than for many
years past. Less than a decade ago they
were about the most prosperous and re-
putedly the best-managed in these provinces.
The unspeakable Gillies-Deakin Coalition,
however, started them on a short road to
ruin by a frantic policy of extension which
consisted in building useless scrag-ends of
line in all directions, according to no possible
system, and apparently for no purpose save
to secure political support. The wild ex-
travagances of the magnificent Speight— in
his own way almost as lavish as Gillies—
completed the wreck. Since Speight’s
fall a succession of mediocrities have pot-
tered with the State’s principal asset, and
have economised in a helpless, shiftless
fashion by postponing repairs and reducing
the smaller class of salaries, and by doing
various other things in an ineffectual sort of
way which utterly fails to meet the emer-
gency. Victoria is urgently in want just
now of a railway expert who is many degrees
above the average—just such a one as New
South Wales possesses. Both provinces, too,
profess to want Federation, and only recently
Premiers Dibbs and Patterson seriously
considered a scheme of commencing the
unification of Australia by a combination of
the two States which they represented,
leaving the others to enter the league at
their leisure. This proposal for making a
small beginning in the great work of unifica-
tion presently dropped out of sight, as all
similar proposals have done. It might,
therefore, be worth while to consider the
advisability of making a still smaller begin-
ning by combining the two railway systems
underonemanagement, and offering Mr. Eddy
the position of commissioner to two provinces
at a salary of, say, £6OOO a year, or whatever
other sum may be considered adequate for
the office.
• •
The difficulties in the way of such a
proceeding are doubtless great, but they are
by no means insuperable. The arrangement
would resemble in some measure the familiar
“ pool ” of the American railroad companies,
only its object would be increased efficiency
and economy, not the fleecing of the public
by enhanced charges. The two provinces, so
far as their assets are concerned, would
enter into a tolerably equal partnership.
Victoria owned, at the close of 1893, about
2950 miles of railway, much of it profitless,
and New South Wales only 2400 ; but the
Victorian lines were built for an average cos
of £2OOO per mile less than those of her
neighbor, so that the capital on which in-
terest has to be paid is almost identical.
Owing to easier gradients the working ex-
penses for each mile run are much less in
Victoria than in New South Wales; the
passenger traffic is also very much larger,
while the goods traffic is practically the
same. Under these conditions the Victorian
system, with equally good management,
would almost certainly be at least as profit-
able as that of New South Wales—in fact,
until the disastrous Gillies-Speight com-
bi nation came upon the country the southern
province showed the better average of the
two, and its present abject condition merely
goes to prove how the finest State property
in Australasia has been wrecked by Duncan,
the boomer, and his associates.
* * m
The best combination which could be de-
vised would be effected by consolidating the
railway debts of the two provinces and
making both equally liable for principal and
interest, and by combining the two systems
with one management, one working staff,
one tariff, one uniform gauge, and one Par-
liamentary head. But this would involve
absolute Federation, and that is a result
which is still apparently very far off, and
which seems to get no nearer as the years go
by. All that can be done in the present is
to effect a species of partnership which may
serve a good purpose until something better
can be arrived at. The rival systems could
be placed under one set of Commissioners,
thus saving a considerable outlay in salaries.
They could be managed, so far as the superior
officials are concerned, by a joint staff,
which affords room for very extensive econo-
mies in the engineering, stores, and other
departments. Now that the boom in railway
construction is over, and the lines are being
extended by a very gradual process, in many
of the railway offices one set of experts could
easily do the work of two, and a large reduc-
tion in the annual salary-list would thus be
effected. The war of rates on the border
could be wholly abandoned, a uniform tariff
being substituted for the two erratic ones
now in force, and by this means much need-
less accountancy could be saved. Also, there
is no reason, save interprovincial jealousies,
why the work of future construction should
not be placed under the control of a joint
parliamentary committee, consisting of an
equal number of representatives from each
province ; and the free pass of every M.L.A.
might be made good over the railways from
the Queensland border to the debateable
land which divides Victoria from South
Australia. This is probably all that could be
done in the meantime ; for while the present
discrepancy exists between the net profits,
and until the Victorian system gets out of
the slough of bankruptcy to which it was
reduced under the Gillies regime, no actual
fusion is possible. But if, as may reason-
ably be expected, the two systems should
become in a few years about equally pros-
perous, there is no reason why they should
not be consolidated, with one staff and one
system of book-keeping, and the proceeds
be equally divided between the two
Treasuries.
* # *
The saving to be effected by such a policy
must, under economical management, be
necessarily large, though no exact estimate
of the amount can be arrived at. The
control of 5000 miles of iron road is doubt-
less a large order, but it is by no means
beyond the powers of one man of command-
ing ability—there are at least two railway
systems of twice these dimensions in exist-
ence under a single control. The success of
the experiment would depend wholly on one
efficient head, and an ample supply of
capable subordinates ; the former is already
available, and the latter can always be found
when the right man looks for them.
Moreover, though the proposed arrange-
ment may be a small matter in the
ultimate unification of Australia, it would
be, at all events, the first practical
step towards an effective union, and the
first step, no matter how trifling it may be,
counts for something. At least 90 per cent.
—probably 99 per cent. —of Australians have
as yet no sentimental affection whatever for a
United Australia ; with them Federation is
a matter of interest and nothing else. If it
can be demonstrated that there is some-
thing substantial to be gained in the
immediate present the almost - forgotten
movement will start afresh, otherwise it will
continue dead until further notice. In the
present case, there is a chance of proving
that something is to be gained, and that
is the most powerful argument which can
be advanced for the cause of Australian
Nationalism.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gag in Prisons.
That bloodless attorney, N.S.W. Minister for
Justice Gould, is being egged on by his subor-
dinates to allow them to thrust a humane gag
into the mouths of gaol prisoners, and the
S.M. Herald publishes a warm eulogy of gag-
ging on general principles in order to remove
any possible hesitation in the matter. The
new gag, it is explained, is not at all a brutal
contrivance. On the contrary, prisoners rather
like it in their mouths than otherwise ; and the
only tiling to be feared is that they will deliber-
ately make themselves objectionable in order to
be gagged. The Herald, however, rather
inconsistently persists in regarding even
bhe improved variety of gag as a punish-
ment ; and propounds the theory that
“ criminals are put in gaol to be punished, and
if they will not conform to the rules made
for their restraint they must be punished again
till they submit.” No penologist, no man
of science, no thinking individual, will nowa-
days assent to this barbarous dogma. Criminal!
are not put in gaol to be punished. They ara
put in gaol partly to prevent them from com-
mitting further crimes, and partly to deter
others from committing crimes. A criminal is
invariably the product of his environment: the
possible saint, born amid vicious surroundings,
almost inevitably becomes vicious : and criminal
responsibility is merely a social fiction. A man
commits crime, or refrains from committing
crime, simply because, being what he is, sub-
ject to all the influences—hereditary, educa-
tional, social—which have played upon him
before and since birth, he cannot possibly do
otherwise. Considering the individual exclu-
sively in relation to himself, and not in his
relation to society, there is no merit in virtue,
no demerit in vice. None but a free agent can
be a responsible agent; and there is no such
thing as a free agent. Breed children in the
gutter, and they will be tainted with the
gutter ; breed children in an atmosphere
morally and physically healthy, and the chances
are that they will also be healthy. Bub the
taint is not to be ascribed to the individual
as guilt, nor its absence as innocence. So far
as the individual is concerned, guilt and
innocence are only the effects of causes
which are completely out of his control.
Society simply praises or condemns, from its
own standpoint, the conduct which profits
or injures it, with a view to the future,
improvement of social conditions. As regards
past social conditions, which have resulted in
past crime, society, not the individual, must
take the responsibility. It has no warrant
whatever to punish the crime which itself
has created only to prevent the crime
which it may create. And, whether the
right to punish be conceded or no, the
use of the gag as a punishment is brutal and
infamous. It is useless to talk of an emascu-
lated gag, which the victims put on with plea-
sure and take off with regret. A gag, to be
effective, must be brutal. And brutality begets
brutality. The lash and the gag are direct
causes of the garrotter, the ravisher and the
murderer. Society reaps as it sows. Treat
your criminals in gaol as wild beasts, and when
you free them they will turn and rend you.
Treat them as men, and you give them the
best possible reason to become men. In argu-
ing for the gag, the Herald is the mouthpiece
of cruel, callous officialdom, careless of the
results of its actions, eager only to quiet its
victims with the least possible pains. Time
and again, even in post-convict days, has New
South Wales heard of the horrid cruelties in-
flicted by the gag in the hands of warders more
criminal than the prisoners whom they tortured
—with the difference that their crimes were
sanctioned by the law, excused by “ respec-
table ” people, and justified by the Herald
in leading articles. But nothing can
justify the gag. If it is used upon an in-
sane criminal, he should be in the hospital or
the asylum; if upon a sane one, the fault
lies with the prison authorities, not with the
prisoner. The cases in which men—not driven
to rebellion by barbarous regulations, or bar-
barous gaolers—conduct themselves so as, in
the estimation of those gaolers, to need the gag,
are few indeed. For those few—causelessly,
obstinately outrageous —the remedy is an hour
or two’s solitary reflection in a padded room.
But the ghastly face, the gaping jaws, the
bleeding mouth, the choking, swollen throat—
this is a spectacle which N.S. Wales, once
having escaped from, should never see again.
If the Minister for Justice listens to the repre-
sentations of his subordinates and permits it,
if the Cabinet as a body supports the Minister
for Justice, The Bulletin hopes that every
honest man in the province will mark the deed
with a black cross against the day of political
reckoning.
-—■—
The Electors of the Vic. Legislative
Council.
Mr. J. B. Patterson, professed to discover
something “ most significant” in the utter coi -
demnation of the land-tax principle by his
friends in the Victorian Legislative Council.
When he considered that the Assembly repie-
sented 239,000 electors, and the Council repre-
sented 170,000, the difference being only 69,000,
Patterson had no further doubt that the
present time is “ inopportune ” for such a tax.
The Council’s conduct in dancing with both
feet on Turner’s Bill was, in Patterson’s idea,
complete evidence of the aforesaid “ inoppor-
tuneness,” inasmuch as more than two-thirds of
the voting power which elected the big land-tax
majority in the Assembly also elected the still
bigger anti-land-tax majority in the Council.
The situation is peculiar. On the surface of
things, it would seem that out of 239,000 adult
male voters in Victoria, a very large majority—
probably about 180,000—want a land-tax,
as evidenced by their votes at last Assembly
election. At the same time, it would
seem that the 170,000 out of these 239,000,
who have also a vote for the election of the
ancient stalactites of the Council, are almost to
a man against the land-tax, judging by the
fact that all their representatives in the Upper
House, save one, were against that measure, and
even that one was none too friendly. And in this
incredible piece of arithmetic Patterson sees
something “very significant.” Yet the ex-
planation is easy enough. Any male elector
may be sent by the people to represent them in
the Assembly; there is no property qualifica-
tion for members, and as they are paid a salary
anybody, rich or poor, is equally eligible.
Consequently, when the people wanted to send
a big majority of land-taxers into the Lower
House they could do it, and they promptly did
it. On the other hand, no man can be a mem-
ber of the Council unless he owns property of
an annual value of ,£IOO, which is equal to a
capital value of at least .£2ooo—therefore
only landholders are eligible. There is
no payment, therefore only rich land-
holders, w’ho don’t need to work for
their living, are eligible. The constituencies
are very large and election expenses are very


heavy—therefore, only very rich landlords can
qualify for a seat in the House of Fossildom.
And no rich landlord wants a land-tax, because
it is a special tax on himself, therefore the
170,000 electors are only authorised to send
anti-land-taxers to the Council—there is no
one else whom they can send. But the fact
that they are not in accord with their Fat Men
misrepresentatives is sufficiently evidenced by
the tact that the same electors sent a very
different crowd to the Assembly, where they
had a free hand. Under these circumstances,
the “significance” which Patterson discovered
is pure bosh. If the qualification for an M.L.C.
was that he must be a Freetrader instead of an
ami-land - taxer, and Victoria consequently
elected a purely Freetrade Council and a
strongly Protectionist Assembly, the fact would
be just as “significant.” Patterson’s assump-
tion that Victoria filled the Council with anti-
laud-tax property-holders because it wanted to
do so, and that its desire to do so is “ signifi-
cant,” is mere gall and misrepresentation. All
the significance there is about the Council
couldn’t be discovered with a kinetoscope.
“A Free Port.”
£. £. Smith and the Argus urge that Victoria
should follow Denmark in the alleged novel
enactment to establish a free port at the metro-
polis while maintaining the protective policy
elsewhere. As Hamburg and Bremen are part
of the German Empire, but remain outside the
Zollverein, it is strange that such ancient
examples of the “free port” should have
escaped high authorities like £. £. Smith and
the Argus. It is, however, significant of
Hamburg’s case that its jurisdiction was re-
stricted in ’B2 to the city and port—to the
exclusion of the Lower Elbe— i.e, the State of
Hamburg. From The Bulletin’s point of
view the proposal for a free port in Hobson’s
Bay is simply an immoral extension of that
most immoral of all the Argus unprinciples,
enunciated at the last election :
The lowest tariff that will bring in tbe highest revenue
The imposition of a tariff for revenue purposes
is the worst of all the bad legislation foisted on
Australia. For the No-tariff parly there is
some show of reason; but for the Revenue-tariff
party, the mule of parties, the barren com-
promise between incompatibles, there is no
argument except brute noise. The revenue-
tariff is a Tory dodge to protect land and
income from just taxation. The Vic. tax of 3d.
per lb. on tea, for instance, taxes practically
every meal of the mass of the community—
while the rich minority, if anything, escapes
more lightly. The only result of this source of
revenue, and others equally unjust, is to post-
pone the day of the land-tax. Duties that are
not protective should be abolished at once, no
hindrance whatever being offered to the goods
we want. The imports we don’t want should
be discouraged. As far as protected industries
are concerned, a free port within a few miles
w’ould become a den of thieves, ready, by any
trick or artifice, to rob the local manufacturer
of his position and prosperity.
Prohibition on the Clutha.
A Maoriland newspaper-man, who was sent
to inspect the prohibition district of Clutha
(M.L.), and report on the working of the great
Cold Tea movement, has published the results
of his investigation. He devoted a number of
days to the search, and visited every township
and hamlet in the prohibited district, and
found drink everywhere. Almost all the old
pubs, sell it; the only change is that instead
of being sold openly as before it is now handed
out secretly in response to winks and secret
signs of intelligence. In addition to the hotels,
a new class of low-grade shanties has sprung
up, the like of which never existed before. As
much liquor goes into the prohibited region as
went there in the post-prohibition period ; this
fact was ascertained from brewers and whole-
sale merchants in Dunedin, who supply the
district, and who are prepared to demon-
strate by their books that their business is as
robust as ever. The police have apparently
given up any serious effort to cope with
the difficulty. It is too vast and too
widespread, and the secret grog-shops rise
up many times faster than they can be sup-
pressed. The drink is already rather worse
than it used to be ; it is sold, as already men-
tioned, in low, shady dens which, in better
days, had no existence ; the licensing revenue—
a very large item—has been lost; and a new
branch of lying has arisen, for when a sus-
picious stranger comes along and asks for drink
every grog-dealer peijures his immoral soul in
a great swear that he knows of no alcohol any-
where in the whole Clutha electorate. Beyond
this, however, nothing has been effected. It is
the old story. Prohibition is about the meanest
and most drunken thing going. A State Drink
Monopoly would do more for the cause of tem-
perance in one year than all the Prohibition on
earth would accomplish in a century.
Tbe “See-Saw Morning Herald.”
The evolution of the S.M. Herald is a painful
but interesting spectacle. Claiming to be the
leading N.S.W. daily newspaper, it is really a
very unwilling follower of the army of Aus-
tralian journalism. Democracy has to drag it
along as a mother drags her reluctant urchin to
the tub. It kicks and squeals, tugging back-
ward with all its might, and doing its best to
show how it resents purification. Away back
in the century, the Herald started business
with a very fine assortment of Fat principles
and platitudes.. It has been gradually shed-
ding them as the years rolled on—just as the
physically fat man sheds his tissue in the
Turkish bath ; but it still retains enough to be
foolish and unpleasant with. And the Herald
is invariably foolish and unpleasant just after
it has been sensible and pleasant. Its Fatness
appears in repentant spasms when it has con-
ceded more than usual to its Democratic
environment, and allowed itself to be dragge 1
forward a whole step —in the same way
as a gouty person gets his twinges after an
unusually good dinner. Within the last day
or two, for example, the Herald has printed
articles pointing out that ascetic religion is bad
morality, and that a comparison of attendance
at Sydney churches and places of amusement
shows that the latter are far more numerously
patronised ; also, a scandalous paragraph im-
plying that angels elope and have twins. It is
very sorry for all these tilings—particularly for
the scandalous paragraph ; and it endeavours to
make up by printing a venomously Fat article
defending the employment of colored labor on
mailboats to which the white taxpayers of Aus-
tralia give hundreds of thousands of pounds in
subsidy. Now, in its thinner moments, the
Herald knows very well that this defence is in-
defensible. It is a standing disgrace to the pro-
vinces of Australia that a great steamship com-
pany should be paid by the white population of
Australia to employ cheap colored labor at an
enormous profit rather than better-paid white
labor at a moderate profit. The P. and O. Co.
will always find patrons among the shoddy
aristocracy to whom black faces convey a
pleasant suggestion of slavery ; but it is in-
tolerable that white Australia should be taxed
to support aliens who cannot mix with us upon
an equal plane of civilisation, while her own
race goes workless and starving. The Herald
suggests that the P. and O. company might
discontinue its service if compelled to discharge
the cheap and reliable Lascar. Australia’s
troubles ! We have always the Orient Com-
pany (which employs white labor exclusively,
and deserves the patronage of every consistent
Democrat) to take up the running. But there
is not the slightest risk that the P. and O. Co.
will do anything so foolish. If Postmaster-
General Cook and his confreres say decisively
that no subsidy will be given to a coloured-
labor steamer, the company will simply, what-
ever its bluff, discharge its Lascars and replace
them with white men. So the sooner they say,
the better. In the meantime, the Herald will
kindly continue to see saw.
Melbourne and Federation.
The Federation meeting in Melbourne Town
Hall, on 23rd Jan., decided for itself these
three fundamentals for Federation :
Protection against the world.
Interprovincial Freetrade.
One-man-one-vote.
The Tory element present held its peace while
these three principles were declared time and
again with riotous cheers. The reception given
to the Premiers of the neighbor-provinces was
most cordial, but it was overshadowed when
Premier George Turner appeared. This was
the first indication of the Democratic tendency
of the meeting. Pressure of business in Par-
liament presently necessitated Turner’s recall
to the lead of the House—but an interjeccor
affected to believe it was fear of the Trades
Hall! Patterson, in his motion of thanks,
referred to Turner’s position, and in reply to a
derisive laugh, said : “ Make no mistake, Mr.
Turner’s heart is in the work.” Such a declara-
tion from the leader of the Opposition anent the
Premier could net be gainsaid, and was en-
dorsed by cheers, but a dramatic illustration of
the pressure of Vie. Parliamentary business
was given by the appearance of a messenger in
the uniform of Parliament, who hurried on to
the platform at the instant, and stopped Pat-
terson himself, giving him a verbal message of
recall. Patterson went on the instant. It
was then observed that Isaacs and Zox,
M.L.A.’s, had also disappeared from the plat-
form. It was a great moment when Chairman
Madden introduced the N.S.W. Premier. Pre-
mier Reid, thanks to Hop, was familiar in face
and figure to 99 per cent, of his audience. But
the voice was a surprise. The childish treble
came almost as an anti-climax to the great
reception given its owner when he came for-
ward. But the surprise was only momentary,
and speaker and audience came to the best
terms with one another. The result was an
actual oration of the old school, peroration and
all complete—
Let us then unite, as I believe vve will, to secure as a
glorious heritage for our children, these grand destinies,
fortified bv a perpetual league and covenant of Australian
patriotism.
The enthusiasm raised by Premier Reid’s
really fine effort prepared the way for an
ovation for Premier Kingston (S.A.). Three
cheers were given for South Australia, and then,
perhaps, it dawned on people how very big
and ungainly C. C. Kingston really was. The
strident voice and jerky utterance struck the
ear as unsympathetically as did the above-
mentioned childish treble. The intensely small
Tory minority felt smaller still when Premier
Kingston announced his resolve to have one-
man-one-vote in the Federal constitution, and
the cry was taken up from every part of the
hall with the unmistakable emphasis of
repeated cheers. Mayor Tucker, of Adelaide,
had been eulogised by his Premier, but proved
the failure of the evening. Stage-fright had
him for its own, and pathetic platitudes were
produced at weary length. In a lucid interval,
however, he declared for internal Freetrade
and Protection against the world, and then the
Town Hall was filled for a minute with cheers.
Trade-Commissioner Larke, from Canada,
proved to be an agreeable and good-humored
old gentleman, and took some delight in the
statement (with Uncle Sam accent) that, while
he could say proudly that he was a Canadian—
not one of his audience could say: “I am an
Australian.” But it was left to Deakin to de-
liver the absolute oration of the evening. His
text was that greater power and greater issues
never lay in the hands of man than were en-
trusted now to two men— Reid and Kingston.
His limpid, hypnotic eloquence lashed the
crowd into enthusiasm. /
Prison Discipline.
For some years past a printing-plant has been
maintained in the gaol at Dunedin (M.L.), and
the better-conducted prisoners have been taught
the trade of type-setting. The experiment was
a very small one, and it was of such short dura-
tion that it produced no definite results. It
was finally discontinued a few weeks ago,
partly because of the remonstrances of the pro-
fessional type-setters, who complained of the
injury done to their trade through the competi-
tion of released felons, and partly owing to
other causes ; and the prisoners will presum-
ably go back to their profitless occupation of
napping stones as heretofore. The attempts
made in other gaols to teach shoe-making, mat-
making, book-binding, and other trades have
mostly fallen through in similar fashion. The
arguments against the system are that it gives
the felon an undue advantage over the honest
man, who was never afforded an opportunity of
learning a trade in his early years ; and that
prison-labor being subsidised out of the public
revenue, and not burdened by any charge
for wages, its products can be sold at a low
rate, whereby the wages of workmen outside
the gaols are seriously diminished. The argu-
ments in favor of it are that it helps to make
the prisons self-supporting, and that in the case
of the first offender who possessed no honest
means of livelihood previous to his conviction,,
the acquisition of a trade renders him, in some
small degree at least, less likely to become a
confirmed criminal and a permanent burden on
the community. The case is one which admits
of no logical compromise. Either the gaols
should be a vast series of workshops, and every
effort should be made to render them not only
self-supporting but a source of absolute profit
to the State—or else the confinees should be
kept absolutely unemployed so far as any useful
or productive labor is concerned. And if the
average prisoner is taught no trade and em-
ployed at no useful occupation, the chances are
so much in favour of him becoming a reliable
and old-established offender that he might
as well be hanged at once. At all events, there
is something wildly irrelevant about existing
Australian gaol management, which produces
nothing but criminals and goes on no defi-
nite policy whatever. There are only two logical
prison systems at present in force, so far as The
Bulletin is aware, and the Australian system
halts feebly about half-way between them. One
of these is represented by a famous reformatory
in the State of New York, where every prisoner
is educated, taught a trade, and turned out, as
far as possible, a civilised being. The other is
seen at its best in certain Belgian prisons,
where every offender is kept in perpetual soli-
tary confinement. The almost invariable result
is hopeless idiocy, which incapacitates the
prisoners from further active law-breaking, and
causes a great diminution in the criminal
statistics. Both systems are remarkably suc-
cessful in their different ways ; they aim at
something definite, and get there to a consider-
able extent. The Australian prison-systems, on
the other hand, aim at nothing definite, and
don’t get there.

The Vic. Assembly has decided to cut down
the salary of future Agents-General from £2150
to £ISOO. This is a good idea on the surface,
but when viewed more carefully it isn’t so
good as it appears, by a long way. Presumably
the province wants an Agent-General—there
isn’t very much for him to do when such an in-
curably lazy man as Gillies can fill the billet,
but still there is a little, and that little wants
to be done well. If the country proposes to
pay according to the value of the ordinary
routine work, £SOO a year would be quite
enough ; if it proposes to get a man with
brains enough to meet the emergency which
crops up once in a few years or so, he probably
couldn’t be got under £2OOO. The most pro-
mising way is to keep up the Agent-General’s
salary and find more work for that gilded
minion to do so that he may earn his oats.
Just now Victoria pays an English bank
£12,500 a year for the toilsome job of doing
certain clerical work in connection with the
public debt. If an Agent-General at £2OOO a
year were to hire £SOO worth of extra clerical
assistance, and also make the clerical assistance
he has work a little harder, he could probably
get through this work very comfortably, and
thus save £12,000 per annum to his bleeding
country. The crude process of retrenchment
by cutting down salaries isn’t always so good
as it looks. If there is anything to be done,
it is often much cheaper to hire a brainy man
at a good salary and make good use of his
brains, than to have two cheap men falling
over each other to no purpose. For that matter
one clever man at, say, £2500 a year could be
Agent-General for all Australia and Tasmania,
and manage the debts of all six provinces as
well. The Agent-General’s department is one
where Federation might commence with much
advantage and with comparatively little diffi-
culty.
English papers that howl for the enforcement
of holiness in State schools are liberally para-
graphing a Mr. Henry White, author of
“ Tales of Crime and Criminals in Australia,”
who “has spent thirty years in the penal
department of Victoria.” Speaking of that
province, Mr. White says :
“ Education lias abolished the bushranger [lt hasn’t
- it is chiefly the railway and the telegraph that have
abolished him.—Ed. B.l —“but for one bushranger or
horse stealer, we have twenty offenders convicted of
forgery and embezzlement; for one veteran convict im-
ported from other lands we have fifty native larrikins.
Crimes of violence to women and children, mostly com-
mitted by young men, are terribly on the increase, and,
in addition, we have a whole army of strolling pilferers,
prostitutes, and drunkards.
As a cure for all this, Mr. White, who is pro-
bably getting old and dodderingly pious, sug-
gests practical Bible-teaching at the State
schools every day in the week. The author of
“ Tales of Crime in Australia ” is distinctly off
in several important particulars. The native
“ larrikin ” is not necessarily a criminal any
more than is the playful young English earl;
crimes of violence against women and children
are not increasing proportionately with the
increase of population ; and this class of crime
is usually perpetrated by elderly men. Crime
is proportionately much the smallest amongst
the native-born population, and in any case the
crime-record in Victoria compares more than
favorably with that of any country where the
Gospel is force-pumped into the children by the
State. In fact, local professional pietists can-
not disguise their mortification because young
Victorians are not conspicuously vicious—they
would be so valuable as a shocking example.
Melbourne Age believes in the land and in-
come-tax, but it believes in it with considerable
difficulty. The following is an example of its
feelings on the subject:
Very much of what Mr. Wynne said in the way of argu-
ment against a tax on land may be at once admitted. . .
For example, no one can deny that the tax which falls on
landowners and mortgagees is a tax on thrift. . . All
accumulated property is the result of thrift somewhere,
and all taxation, of whatsoever shape, must fall, in tha
last resource, on property.
In a certain far-off sense this is correct. For
instance, when a bank-director grants himself a
big overdraft and never repays it, that acquisi-
tion is the result of thrift on the part of the
ruined depositors. And when—as happened in
a good many instances—some old-time digger
bought a cheap allotment in ancient Melbourne,
and then was surprised years afterwards to find
that the forgotten property had made him rich,
that was also an evidence of thrift—on the part
of the people who built a big city during tha
land-holder’s absence. Most accumulations
are an evidence of thrift, either on the part of
the men who have them or on the part of the
men from whom the present owners stole them.
Other accumulations show that the owner
thriftlessly gambled in a sweep and won the
big prize, or that he was a bookmaker, or struck
a rich reef, or something. Si ill, in the main,
the Age is right, so far as it goes. A big for-
tune is, in most cases, an evidence that the
money was dishonestly acquired in the first
place, and then the owner’s thrift—or that of
his father, or his uncle, or somebody else-
enabled him to keep it when it was acquired.
And the want of a big fortune generally shows
that the man who doesn’t own it lacked either
the thrift, or the dishonesty, or both. There-
fore, a tax on big capitalists may be fairly set
down as a tax on thrift, good luck, brains, dis-
honesty, meanness, and various other qualities.
The Age, however, which is democratic with
difficulty, mentions the thrift only, and entirely
omits the other aspects of the case. No one
ever made a big fortune by thrift alone.

There was neither sitting nor standing-
room, nor any other form of space whatever, when
the Brough-Bouoicault Co. made its re-appearanoe
at Sydney Lyceum. The regular Hebraic Satur-
day-night audience was there in full force and in
the height of fashion ; there were at least two
acres of undressed back and shoulders in the d.c.,
and the male part of the crowd represented all the
pawnable assets of Sydney. When the curtain
went up, and Brough and Titheradge appeared as
two feeble old persons tottering down a flight of
steps, the applause lasted three consecutive
minutes, and Mrs. Brough got an equally enthu-
siastieweception. The new production, “Sowing
the Wind,” is a drama of that bygone era when
men wore sea-green pants that were braced up at
one end and strapped down at the other till they
threatened to fly asunder at the knees. It is ba?ed
chiefly on the foolishness of Brough, as a sour,
black-coated, black-hatted person who muddles
his friend’s business with the best possible
intentions and the worst possible results. Years
before the story opened, when Titheradge was
young and foolish, he formed “ a relationship ”
with a financially undesirable young person, but
his sour friend made inquiries of a certain wicked
lord who assured him that the young person was a
wholly worthless character. Brough accepted the
wicked lord’s statements without question, and
Titheradge accepted Brough’s assurances right
off, and the lady was abandoned, and went to the
bad with the wicked lord, and then died and was
forgotten. Some 25 years afterwards Titheradge’s
adopted son falls in love with her (and Tither-
adge’s) daughter, and the same sour friend goes
out to make inquiries of the same wicked lord,
and the wicked lord, who wants the daughter to
go to the bad with him as her mother did years
before, cheerfully assures Brough that she is also
a thoroughly worthless character. Then the old
man, who has gained no wisdom in the past
quarter-century, goes away contentedly on two
spidery legs and makes a whole lot of unforeseen
complications.
• • •
The drama moves a little slowly now and
then, but the magnificent scene in act 111., where
Mrs. Brough casts off her unrecognised father and
his fellow-dodderer, and declares war against the
whole world of snuffle and unoharitableness, more
than compensates. Her acting in this scene is a
whirlwind of sustained passion, and the two
venerable pedants go down like straw before it,
until one of them has to carry the other off in a
state of insensibility. Titheradge’s lost daughter
is an exceedingly fine piece of work all
through, and well worthy of the actress’s
high reputation. Cecil Ward fills his char-
acter as the adopted son respectably. Mr.
Carey makes an excellent figure as the wicked lord
—an old, hairless miscreant of the deepest dye, and
the other characters are satisfactory, especially a
certain cackling maiden, in which part the clever
Miss Grace Noble strikes out an entirely new
line. One of the firm’s recent importations, Mr.
Dorrington, makes his first appearance as a rat-
hunting baronet, and promises well, if his blank-
ness of countenance is high art and not nature.
“Sowing the Wind” is a good play allround,
with several strong morals in it, the most con-
spicuous of which is that a moral old ass is the
worst kind of ass going. Everybody feels this,
and when the wicked lord rises up, in Act IY.,
and addresses Brough on the subject in a few
well-ohosen remarks, the audience expresses its
cordial approval.
*» * *
George Rignold’s pantomime, at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney), has been revised and altered
and re-decorated, and the new edition is going
strong on the home stretch and still full of running.
Almost everything is new except the fire-ballet,
which is still the prettiest effect of the show.
** * *
The alleged Comedy Co., which burst last
week at the School of Arts, hasn’t been heard
from since, and the Criterion remains shut. The
habit of being shut is growing upon this theatre,
and considering that it is one of the prettiest
houses in Sydney its ill-luck is unaccountable.
** * *
The last nights of Bland Holt’s “ Span of
Life” are announced, but its successor is not yet
definitely elected. The “ Span ” has done the
best and longest run achieved by any melodrama
in Sydney for years past, and business even now
is almost, if not quite, as good as it was the first
week.
** * *
“ Charley’s Aunt” is still doing the Sydney
suburbs, with great success. It has lately been
heard of in the wilds of Burwood and Granville.
To-night (Thursday) it appears at Liverpool,
where the population consists mostly of super-
annuated citizens in the various asylums. It
Joes Campbelltown on Friday, Camden on Satnr-
iay, and North Shore (Sydney) on Monday.
#* * *
Woodloch’s circus will be further adorned
lext Monday with a water pantomime, 50,000
fallens of refreshing deluge being poured into th e
ring for the purpose. This will bo good enough if
the weather has quite cleared up by that time; if it
hasn’t Woodloch had better postpone the new
effect. Sydney has had enough unavoidable water
pantomime of late.
* m * *
Frank M‘Kiernan, known to very many
Melbourne people as the cheerful purser of the
excursion steamer Ozone, is compelled by the
affliction of failing eyesight to quit his breezy
billet. The management has arranged to give a
farewell benefit to this respected servant of the
pleasure-seeking public. It will take the form of
a moonlight trip per Ozone on Feb. 14, when
anyone desirous of doing Mao a good turn may
purchase tickets for his little nocturnal outing
instead of sending him a valentine. For all par-
ticulars apply to the advt.
* * * *•
One hot night last week a well-mixed and
particularly well-managed show was given at
Melb. Opera House, by way of a send-off for Mrs.
Edouin Bryer, who sails for London in the Oro-
tava. It was a benefit; a good old-fashioned
ben.—not a “grand testimonial,” and a comfort-
able cheque came out of it.
* « •
Mrs. Edouin Bryer, whose portrait is
given elsewhere—she was not professionally known
as Bryer during her husband’s lifetime—is asso-
ciated with the early history of the Melbourne
stage. As Miss Lizzie Naylor, a blushing damsel
of 16, she got her first whiff of the Yarra some 39
years ago, when she came from England under
consignment to her aunt, Mrs. Avins. Mrs.
Avins enjoyed the distinction of being the first
lady to appear at the first theatre or hall erected
in Melbourne (a.d. 1840), upon the site of the Bull
and Mouth Hotel, Bourke-street. Miss Naylor’s
relative introduced her to manager Coppin, at the
Theatre Royal, and there the new arrival made her
debut in a burlesque, “Once Upon a Time There
Lived Two Kings,” young Henry Harwood being a
nimble item of the production. In ’63, fair Lizzie
married one of the Edouin (Bryer) brothers, whose
sister meanwhile had become Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis, and with the Lewis company she did a
long tour through the East. Back again in Mel-
bourne, she resumed work at the Royal, and,
later, joined Lyster’s musical company at the Opera
House. Those were working times in the purfesh,
when an actress would appear as Hamlet’s mother
(one of Mrs. B.’s best characters) at 7 o’clock, and
pop up in extravaganza three hours later. In
the original never-to-be-forgotten production of
“ Struck Oil,” the part of Mrs. Stofel fell to Mrs.
Bryer, and she filled it in all the Williamsoman
revivals of that lucrative entertainment. When the
firm took over the Royal, in ’BO or thereabouts, Mrs.
Bryer retained the position of “ first old woman,”
which she had made her own before she ceased to
be quite young off the stage. In the useful
capacity of a plump matron, sporting always a
cap and apron, Mrs. Bryer figured in most of the
successful dramas of the Firm’s halcyon period.
Also, she has impersonated skittish old women, at
times, in comic opera, and her final appearance
was to be made, on Tuesday of this week, in “ The
Judge,” a farcical comedy. Time is dealing
kindly with this respected player of many parts,
and when she comes back from foggy Britain, as
she doubtless will, there should be plenty more
professional engagements for “Ma” Bryer to
undertake.
* * * *
At the Princess’s there was a good demand
for the last nights of “Beauty and the Beast,”
despite a high temperature and a profound odour
of paint in the vicinity. The entrances to the
auditorium were hung with flags of all nations,
and disguised in what the daily newspapers call a
“ gala dress,” but the painter and decorator could
be smelt from behind the bunting, and a dread of
colic pervaded the air. This week the work of
making Australia’s most beautiful theatre more
beautiful than ever will be carried on with closed
doors. On Saturday (2nd Feb.) the panto, from
Sydney Lyceum is to appear at the Princess’s in a
somewhat altered form. Shapely Marie Luella
joins the new company, and the show promises to
intoxicate the senses and “ excite the risibilities of
the audience.”
* * * *
“Aladdin” romps along at the Royal.
Last nights have commenced, but the actual date
of Maggie Moore’s departure is not mentioned, as
yet.
* * * *
“The Foundling,” after a severe tussle
with the weather, began to flourish at Melbourne
Bijou. It has to withdraw, in favor of a noble
earl, on Wednesday night, however, and will pro-
bably be superseded by “ A Mint of Money,” on
Saturday. The last-named farce, which is new to
Australia, and includes a bevy of 12 female char-
acters, will run for two weeks. By that time the
Dacres will be ready to open their campaign.
* * * *
Yarmouth Revels, in aid of distressed
actors and the Children’s Hospital, are to take
place at the Bijou to-night (Thursday) and to-
morrow. The demand for seats at advanced prices
is a complete vindication of manager’s Stuart’s
conduct in this matter, and nothing that the Earl
of Yarmouth may perpetrate can alter the fact
that charity is covering a tony advertisement for
the theatre. Meanwhile, Melbourne’s amaze-
ment at what his lordship has volunteered to do is
almost as great as the general desire to see him do
it. Reason totters under the contemplation of
Yarmouth’s programme. He will inaugurate the
proceedings by appearing in “ Our Bitterest Foe,”
and then pass on rapidly to one of the name-
parts in “ A Pair of Lunatics.” His appetite
for acting being whetted, Lord Yarmouth wjlj
next undertake a funny character, including
extras, in W. S. Gilbert’s “ Creatures of Im-
pulse.’’ The extras, or interpolations, are as
follows : —Song, “Tommy Atkins,” by His Lord-
ship; duet, “Doddle Doddle,” by Lord Yar-
mouth and Miss Hardy ; special Yarmouth dance,
His Own invention, and entitled “ The Moth and
the Candle,” by the Noble Earl and Miss Florrie
Green ; also a whistling solo by the Youthful
Peer, accompanied by the orchestra. Upon the
completion of the aforesaid arduous tasks, the
Hereditary Legislator will entwine himself with
48 yards of silk, specially imported from Paris,
and perform a serpentine dance. This item is
reserved for a grand climax, because it is
very exhausting, and Lord Yarmouth usually
faints freely between the encores. But
should his strength permit, the high-spirited
nobleman—for, hang it 1 doncherknow, The
Bulletin must give him credit for pluck—
may be induced to throw in a neat little speech.
Lord Yarmouth is an intelligent, active person-
age behind the scenes, where he desires to be
known as “Peter,” and for the sake of his Gracious
Queen, not to mention the existing Constitution,
The Bulletin hopes for the best.
** * *
The Victoria Hall duly opened in opposi-
tion to the Oxford last Saturday. Also, Harry
Rickards has leased the Opera House, and will
put a strong variety-show in possession on Feb-
ruary 9th.
** * *
One of the most promising of young Aus-
tralian actors is Harry Roberts, whose portrait
appears in this issue of The Bulletin. He has
the faculty of versatility fully developed—an
essential element on the Australian stage, and in
proof thereof he has already run the whole gamut
of characters, from Shakespeare to pantomime.
Although worse in some than in others he has
never yet been credited with a thoroughly bad
performance. Mr. Roberts, who has lived 27
years, inherits the stage instinct his father,
R. R. Roberts, haying made a speciality of
Jefferson’s parts, whilst his mother, Miss Polly
Leake, is remembered by old playgoers as a highly
capable actress. Young Roberts made his first
bow at the green age of four, when he appeared at
Calcutta in company with General Tom Thumb.
His next appearance was at the age of 18, when
he played Horatio to G. R. Ireland’s Hamlet,
on a tour through Tasmania. Proceeding steadily
in the profession, Roberts was first hailed
as a capable player when he represented Beli-
sarius in Plain Bill Holloway’s production of
“ Cymbeline,” at Melb. Princess’. He was for
two or three years a useful member of Bland
Holt’s company, and has also assisted in many of
George Rignold’s productions. His opportunity
came when Maggie Moore was in search of a
leading man, capable of taking a hand in melo-
drama, burlesque, comedy, opera, pantomime, and
tragedy, as occasion required. Roberts confidently
undertook this varied order, and for two years has
fulfilled it to Miss Moore’s abundant satisfaction.
In addition, he has more than once efficiently
stage-managed Maggie’s productions, and Lizzie
Stofel is not likely to leave him behind when she
finally quits Australia for Yankeeland—if such a
disaster ever happens. Roberts’ Widow Twankey,
in Melb. Theatre Royal pantomime this year, is as
good a conception of this rusty character as many
that have come from the other side of the water,
but it is a pity to see so intelligent an actor spend-
ing his intellectual substance in such riotous
absurdity.
At Poverty Point .
At “ Bill’’ Hind’s funeral there attended as one of the
mourners “ Old Joe ” Rayner, of the Rayner’s Minstrels
of the fifties and subsequently leading man in support of
Marie Prdvost, who toured Australia in the sixties.
Marie Luella, the beautiful Beast in Melb. Princess’s
panto., despite her Franco-Italian stage-name, is Irish
from the top of her wig to the painted heel of her satin
slippers. Her accent gives her away; “Oi love huurr’’
is pure Dublin. Marie adopts May YohC’s trick of turning
up the end of one leg of her striped pants over the knee
when she sings her “Plantation Song.” It is a simple
dodge, but the variation of length of limb revealed is
peculiarly and insinuatingly “ fetching."

Mrs. Edouin Bryer relates that once when she acted
Ophelia at Bendigo the supply of ladies was short to help

Mrs. Onehunga Yates’ promised lecturing tour seems to
hang fire, though an English paper says that Smythe in
person has arranged to exhibit the lady.
Undoubtedly the way to make a pantomime profitable
is that adopted by George Rignold at Sydney Majesty’s,
where a stiff sum was put into brilliant mechanical effects
and striking scenery, while salaries are kept as moderate
as possible. The first cost of scenery and mechanism is
the whole coat, and when that is repaid their share of the
takings is clear profit; but big-salaried “stars” eat up
the popular shillings almost as fast as they attract them
—and faster when the audience begins to tire. No panto,
can have a long run with heavy initial outlay and heavy
current expenses; and discreet managers choose rather
to cut current expenses.

with Miss Ella Russell, Mr. Sims Reetes, Mr. Ben Davies,
Signor Foli, MM. Woolf and Slivinski, and the Meister
Glee Singers. Mrs. Green .sang “The Worker” and a
“Spanish Bolero,” and won a double recall on each occa-
sion.
The “ Continentals ” held on Wednesday and Saturday
evenings, on Adelaide Jubilee Exhibition grounds, have
grown very popular. There is no restraint on the patrons,
who can drink, smoke, promenade, chat, or flirt as they
like.
London Daily Telegraph's Paris correspondent writes,
on 20th Sept.: Madame (Frances) Saville has made her
diShut, at the Op6ra Comique, under the most favorable
circumstances, in a work which had already pleased a
Parisian public, but which was new to a large proportion
of those present. It is 18 years since “ Paul et Virginie ”
performed the miracle of saving from disaster one of the
numerous ThCAtre Lyrique enterprises, but it has never
since been revived. The libretto was cleverly adapted
from Bernardin de Saint Pierre’s novel by MM. Barbier
and Carr 6, and the music was written by M. Victor Masss,
who is best known in England by hi* one-act trifle, “Les
Noaes de Jeannette ” and his “ Reine Topaze." He was
an accomplished musician, and personally he was much
esteemed; but, though his melodies are elegant and his
concerted pieces well-written, he never rises to a very
high level. Evan the success of “ Paul et Virginie ” was
iu great measure to be attributed to the excellence
of the original interpretation, and if this prove to be suffi-
cient it is safe to pregnosticate an equal success for the
present revival. In, the first place, Madame Saville looks
the part to perfection, and this is no small recoinmei da-
tion, but this is not ail. Fortunately, h;r voice is as
fresh and pure as her appearance. She prepo-sessed her
hearers in her favour by her share in the love duet sumr
by Virginia and Paul when they run on the stage, shel-
tered from the rays of the tropical sun by the huge
banana-leaf which they use as a parasol ; and in her
plaintive rendering of the air “ Pardonnez Lui,” warbled
by her as she intercedes for the slave M6ala, Madame
Saville showed how artistically she can sing a sustained
melody. In the more brilliant portions of the r6le,
notably in the aria supposed to be sung by
Virginie in Paris and heard by Paul in the lie Bour-
bon, the lady was equally successful. The idea of this
duet, hy the way, sung by two people in different parts of
the world, is probably the boldest that ever entered a
librettist’s brain. However, it is quite effective, and
here, as in all the rest of his role, M. Clement is as ideal a
Paul in appearance as Madame Saville is a Virginie worthy
to be his partner. In spite of the extreme length of the
performance, the audience was enthusiastic through' ut.
It ought to be added that Madame Saville’s diction is as
good as her singing, and those who did not know her his-
tory were surprised to learn that she was not French-
Seeing how hypercritical our friends here are, this is no
Blight compliment.
Little Ethel, the clever infant trapezist of Woodloch’s
Circus, while doing a trapeze act at Sydney on Salur-
day afternoon last, wound her silk sash so lightly
round the bar that she was left hanging head-down and
almost suffocated. It was some moments before her pre-
dicament was noticed, and she was getting black in the
face when her father shinned up the hoisting rope, and
after desperately tearing at the sash for several minutes,
while he hung by his legs on the trapeze and worked
head-down, he had to get a ktdfe sent up, and with
great difficulty managed to cut the tough silk away in
bits. It was quite ten minutes before the child was
released from her trying fix, and her father cut his hand
badly in the process. Then the plucky youngster went on
with her performance amid great applause.

The story told in a recent Bulletin of the chloroformed
goat in “Pygmalion ” which insisted on recovering at the
wrong moment, suggests another episode in connection
with the same comedy. Many years ago, in Maoriland,
Charles Burford was Pygmalion, and Clara Stephenson,
wdth a bad cold, played Galatea. All went well until
Cynisca drew back the curtains and bade the scn’ptor
gaze upon his famous statue, when that masterpiece was
found sneezing furiously in its recess. There was a
horrible pause. The audience laughed frantically. The
statue doubled itself up in an agony of “ A-chew," and
straightened itself again, and then sneezed convulsively
some more, and felt for its handkerchief, and then the
curtain came down suddenly, and Burford was heard
remonstrating with the masterpiece, and the masterpiece
was heard saying “ A-chew ” till further notice.

The new Sydney Trades Hall, at the
corner of Dixon and Goulburn streets, was
formally opened by Minister for Labor Garrard
on Jan. 26. The foundation-stone was laid by
Gov. Carington in Jan,, ’BB, but various difficul-
ties have delayed the werk. The Govt, gave
.£6OOO to secure a site, and the building has cost
about =29000 additional. The large hall seats,2ooo
people, and there are many additional lodge-
rooms. The trades societies are to be congratu-
lated on the possession of such a handsome and
commodious edifice.
Sydney papers of Jan. 29 print a short
wired account of the capture by the police of an
alleged offender named Rhody Sheedey, which
has a thrill of derring-do about it. Sheedey was
in the yard of an hotel at Forbes, a far-back
N.S.W. township, when two troopers rode up.
One dismounted, and came at Sheedey pistol in
hand ; the other stayed at the gate, which was
closed, and held his mate’s horse across it. The
trooper walked confidently up, thinking Sheedey
an easy prey, as the yard was surrounded by a
high fence, and the sft. spiked gate was the
lowest part of it. As soon as Sheedey saw the
constable, he jumped on his horse, drove the
spurs in, and flew gate and horse like a bird.
The constables rode after him, and, after a four-
miles’ race, one of them got within 40 yards of
Sheedey and called on him to stop. Sheedey
laughed and kept on ; and the trooper, fearing he
would jump another high fence close by, an<j get
clear, fired his revolver and wounded Sheedey,
whom he afterwards caught and arrested. This
is the police yarn, adding that the constable
“aimed at Sheedey’s shoulder,” which is pretty
tall—after a four miles’ race, with the mark 40
yards away. Anyhow, Rhody Sheedey’s pluck
deserves to have been shown in a better cause.
His luck seems to have been bad ever since he
could toddle. At school (where, by the way, he
sat on the same form as a noted N.S.W. literary
horseman) he was the typical bad boy, always
getting into trouble for squashing the master’s
nose with a tomato, or something of the sort. The
public, however, will flatly refuse to believe that
so game and clever a horseman hasn’t a lot of good
points concealed about him.

There is nothing very remarkable about the
balance-sheet of the Assets Realization Go. (Sydney), save
the strange anomaly, of a co. existing for the express pur-
pose of buying up other people’s assets, and being
unable to realise its own. The phenomenal amount of
£43,301 has been considered a necessary allowance for bad
debts and depreciation. The unpaid calls are also
seriously large, forming, as they do, nearly an
eighth of the co.’s actual capital. Total receipts from
realisation of assets, £24,000; from calls, £l7,soo—thus
liabilities have been reduced by £34,154. The year’s new
business has yielded a profit of £1325 95., whilst expenses
of management are set down at £1365 9s. 10d., so that the
00. has been almost self-sustaining. The history of the
concern, however, will be some melancholy satisfaction to
those people who have lost money in financial institu-
tions, etc., as nearly all its shareholders are sharp men of
banking and building society experience, who, when they
formed the co., thought they had a particularly good
thing in being practically the only snappers-up of assets
on anything like a big scale.

Amidst liquidations, bankruptcies and other
barrenness, the Australian Gaslight Co., Sydney, flourishes.
Net profits for past half-year (including last year’s
balance), £57,572, of which £51,134 goes to pay a 9s. per
share div.—equal to 15 per cent, per annum on the shares
paid to £6. Small wonder the co. is anxious to secure the
monopoly of the lighting of Sydney by electricity, when
gas, which is dearer to manufacture, can be made to show
such returns. If Sydney Council had control of the gas-
supply, it is safe to assume the price to consumers would
be reduced by fully a fifth. The ratepayers would thus
be the beneficiaries instead of, as at present, M.L.O. Fat-
inen and absentee English investors.
* • * «
The Queensland National Bank shows no speci-
ally interesting symptoms. It earned £11,374 in profits
for the half-year, against £9797 for the half-year before,
and £18,333 for the one preceding. Compared with a
year ago, therefore, the profits have decreased by £2OOO
while the capital has increased by £llO,OOO, so the change
is not distinctly an improvement. The total deposits
have also decreased considerably, and as the Government
account is getting steadily larger, the falling-off is evi-
dently in private business. The cash reserve has in-
creased by £200,000, which is the only definite sign of
renewed strength noticeable anywhere. The auditors’
certificate is drawn up in the usual beautifully-guarded
fashion, and states that “in our opinion ” the
balance-sheet truly exhibits “ the affairs of the
bank as shown by the books." Which certificate com-
mits the auditors to nothing, and explains nothing,
and especially avoids explaining the one thing which the
public most want to know. Everybody is fully aware that
the balance-sheet agrees w'ith the books ; what people are
mostly afraid of in banking matters nowadays is that the
hooks don’t by any means agree with the present value of
the securities. Meanwhile, public opinion of the Q.N.B.
is expressed by the usual quotation for its shares (£4 ss.
paid up): “Buyers, Is.” Considering that the dividend
of 3 per cent, per annum amounts to over 2s. 6d. per
share, this figure shows a painful lack of public confidence
~or something
« « « •
The City Bank of Sydney remains in just
about the sime situation it has occupied for a year
or so past. Such small changes as there are in its figures
are, on the whole, not much for the better. During the
last year £41,000 more capital has been ealled up, but the
half-year’s profits (£10,004) are £1464 less than those of
last half, and £730 less than those of the one before. The
div. has gone down from six per cent, to five. The City
follows its usual policy in paying away all its profits in
divs. For the half-year ending Dec., ’93, it saved £317
with which to “ strengthen its position." Next half-year
it paid away £145 more than it earned. This time, after
paying its reduced dividend, it has a joyous £4 9*. 2d.
left to carry to profit and loss. Comparing its figures now
with those of a year ago, the reserve has decreased by
£119,000; the deposits have decreased by £35,000; the
note circulation has decreased by £90,000 (this is mainly
owing, however, to the expiration of the Bank Issue Act);
the item, “Bills discounted and other debts due to the
bank,” has diminished by £221,000 ; the profits and the
div. have both decreased, as already mentioned; and
though £41,000 of new capital has been called up, the
cash on hand has only increased by £12,000.
These are the principal depressing features
about the City Bank of Sydney. As a contra, the cash on
hand is over 25 per cent, of the total liabilities to the
public, whereas a year ago it was only 21J per cent. In
other respects there is a solemn fading away, but in this
one direction the institution is distinctly stronger than it
was at the beginning of ’94.
• • v «
Another downward-looking document is the
balance-sheet of the A.J.S. Bank. The half-year’s profits
at Dec. ’93, were £51,000 ; for half-year ending June, ’94,
they were £32,000 ; for last half-year they were under
£22,000. The 5 per cent. div. has dropped to 4 per cent.,
and even that leaves only £4OO to be carried forward. In
the first 18 months of the new bank’s existence it accumu-
lated a reserve of £45,000, and on the present occasion it
adds £1590 to it as a matter of form, said amount being
mostly taken from profit and loss. Considering that the
paid-up capital has increased from £700,000 to £1,100,000,
this steady falling-away in the earnings is remarkable,
and if it is continued at the same rate, by the time the
A.J.S. has a capital of £1,500,000 it will earn nothing at
all. The bank has £230,000 more cash on hand than it
had six months ago. It has reduced its reconstructed
deposits by £260,000 and thus makes a considerable
saving in interest; its new fixed deposits have diminished
—another saving in interest; its current accounts, which
don’t bear interest, have largely increased ; so has its
note circulation, and—as already mentioned—its capital.
All tliese favorable circumstances, along with a reduced
rate of expenditure, should have produced bigger profits,
yet the profits get smaller, and nothing in the sheet
throws any light on this unaccountable circum-
stance. If the new A.J.S. Bank, at the time of its pros-
perity, a year ago, had been exercising its un-
doubted right te buy up its own depreciated
■deposit-receipts at market-value that would, doubt-
less, account for everything; £lOO,OOO worth of old
inabilities, bought up and extinguished at, say, 16s. in the
£, would yield a net profit of £20,000, which would swell
the figures considerably. And if the A.J.S. had since
abandoned this practice the falling-off would be intel-
ligible. Otherwise it is wholly unintelligible.
The A.J.S. balance-sheet is the clearest, fullest,
and most definite document of its kind issued in Aus-
traliaf-* But even the most definite kind of balance-sheet
doesn’t throw much light on the problem now under
discussion.

lian life offices to issue its balance-sheet for the year. It
