£lOO,OOO in '93, hut these things are only what might be
reasonably expected in a time of profound and solemn
depression. The rate of interest has also declined some-
what—another circumstance which might be expected.
On the other hand, the funds now amount to the hand-
some figure of £1,190,548; the interest received from
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
neat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
ithe removal of this imperfection; and now, after
'long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
investments more than covers all death claims ; and the
arrears of interest are set down at the very small sum of
£lBOO, which speaks well for the security of the invest-
ments. The Widows' Fund is the first office to show
separately the interest in arrear and the interest accrued
but not yet due, and it is to he hoped that this system,
which affords a valuable criterion of the safety of the in-
vestments, will be generally followed.
Another highly commendable circumstance is
that the properties acquired by foreclosure are distin-
guished from those purchased in the ordinary course of
business for use as offices. Properties obtained by fore-
closure of mortgages generally mean bad or dubious in-
vestments, and where the entire freehold estate of the
society is lumped together policy-holders are wholly
ignorant as to how much of their money has “ gone
wrong,” or, rather, is in danger of going wrong to a
greater or less extent. The total foreclosures of the
Widows’ Fund only amount to a little over £20,000 in all
—an exceedingly small amount compared to the total of
the investments. Here, also, Tub Bulletin hopes that
other societies will follow suit. The only bad feature in
the sheet is the expense account, per cent, of the
total premiums going in charges. Last year the society
spent barely 22 per cent., under this heading, and the
difference is serious. The board ought to go away to a
cool spot on a lonesome mountain side and think very
hard over this item. There is nothing else in the report
to which any objection can be taken, but this one entry
wants to be thought over until further notice.
» » * *
The City Mutual Fire Insurance Co. pays a 10
per cent. div. and a handsome bonus to police-holders,
and ends the year with £320 to the good. This co. has
been 17 years in existence. In the first five years it
earned nothing in particular save a loss of £3700. During
the remaining 12 years it straightened up sufficiently to
wipe off the loss, accumulate a reserve of £lO,OOO, and
pay £15,000 in divs. In all that period it has only dis-
puted one claim, which is a record for liberality and
fair-dealing. The point which the directors ought
to think over now is that if they paid a smaller div. the
reserve-fund, which is a most important feature, would
get along a deal faster, and the City Mutual would
become a stronger concern than it now is.

♦ * » *
The Perpetual Trustee Co. of Sydney, an insti-
tution which was founded in order that the old house-
father might die happy with a consciousness that his
estate would be honestly administered, and that the
wicked s ilicitor wouldn’t get away with all his worldly
goods and leave his family penniless, is making good pro-
gress with its mission. Business is steadily increasing—
so much so that the co. is able to spend £l5OO in paying a
6 per cent, div., to add £lOOO to the reserve, and have
£594-still on hand. The pity of it is that business doesn’t
improve even faster than it is doing. The pathetic dying
father has to choose between (1) leaving his property
under his wife’s management, in which case her
second husband generally gets away with it; (2) leaving it
in trust with some personal friend, who generally spends
it ; (3) leaving it in trust with a solicitor, who devours it
and becomes a 11 Gent One,” and is struck off the rolls;
or (4) appointing a trustee co. his executor. And the
latter device, in the absence of a Public Trust Office, is so
much safer than any other that the man who leaves his
widow to the tender mercies of a lawyer or an bid friend
deserves to have no rest in Ids grave, and to be an
unquiet ghost on the solicitor’s doorstep until the end of
time.

The North Coast S.N. Co. (Sydney) has declared
a 6 per cent, div., which is one better than last year ; but
this is at least partly due to a 10 per cent, reduction on
the wages of the co.’s captains, officers, and office staff.
The div. absorbs £3652, and the small balance of £B9 is to
be carried forward. The co.’s steamers, which are valued
at something like £70,000, could do with a little writing
down. However, having in view l the partial working of
the North Coast railway, and the low freights offering,
the co. has done better than might have been expected,
and the shareholders can go on their way rejoicing—for a
time, at any rate. ' > '•

Newcastle Wallsend Coal Co. pays a div. of £1
per share for the half-year, totalling £lO,OOO. Last year
this co. reduced the miners’ hewing rate ; this year it has
attacked their yardage rate ; and a motion for acceptance
of the terms now' offered was only carried by one vote at
the miners’ meeting last week. , A ballot increased the
majority to 21, so there will be no strike at present.

tons lead (£231,290), worth together £1,050,593. Nett

That solid little institution, the Ballarat Bank*
ing Co., is one of the few institutions, whose report
contains a hard and fast binding certificate from the
auditors. These gentlemen do not say the balance-sheet
is correct ” according to the accounts,” but that “the
above statement of accounts reflects the true position of
the bank.” There is no “in our opinion,” or “ according
to the books,” or any other little quibble. Accountant
Morrison, who is responsible for this, has given a notable
example.

Maoriland seems to suffer more than usually
from Fatman malice. The other day it was cabled to
England that a big deficit w'as expected in March, and
there was great commotion among stockholders. The
Treasurer now asserts that there will be a gross surplus
of over £350,000 ; a fact which the enemies of triumphant
democracy will doubtless do their best to suppress.
* * * *
Interesting calculation by commercial-editor
Thody, of the Argus, re the fall in prices. The ’94 exports
of Vic. are shown at ’9l prices:—
To show the laxness with which the Victorian
Income-tax Bill is compiled, it is quite possible for any
man with (say) £lOOO a year, “derived from personal
exertion,” to appoint, his wife his secretary at £2OO a
year, and his children (say 3) at £2OO a year each as his
a genii, and thus mop up all his income bar £2OO, which is
exempt. The revenue in such a case would not benefit a
copper.
• * • •
The Royal Bank of Queensland produces
another balance-sheet which is calculated to make some
of its fellow “ reconstructs” green with envy. The lead-
ing feature about the Royal is the steadiness with which
it grinds out the same amount of profit (about
£6500), and pays the same 2J per cent, div., and
carries the same amount to reserve at the end
of each “ half.” In the present instance £3750
goes in divs., £2OOO to reserve, making it £27,000, and
£3OOO to contingency fund, to provide in advance for any
possible depreciation of securities. The Royal i» one of
the very few banks which make it a rule to pay awav only
about half the profits, however small they may be, in
divs., and the pity is that this system is not more gener-
ally followed. Also, its reserve is invested in Govt, securi-
ties, so that there is a fair chance of finding it when wanted.
The business keeps up remarkably well, and there are no
leisures in the report which suggest weakness. Every-
thing shows that the Royal could have ridden out the
storm of ’93 with perfect ease, and that its suspension
was merely the result of groundless panic at a time when
panic was in the air and everybody lost his head.
• • * *
Australian butter, writesTHEßuLLETiN’sLondon
correspondent, is bringing such low prices now that
foreign margarine-makers are in danger of losing their
hold on the London market. The only thing is that the
margarine is much more palatable than some of the
“dairy butter” which has come to hand, and which is
rancid and evil-smelling. If Australian farmers are not
more careful they will rapidly lose their good name for
butter-making.
* * * *
Estate-agent Ham called the attention of the
Vic. Legislative Council to the “injustice” of income-
taxing money which goes to pay interest on a mortgage
upon the victim’s dwelling-house. “ The mortgagee also
paying on the interest received, the property would be
taxed twice,” said the sapient Ham, and never a Coun-
cillor arose to tell him he was talking gammon. A man
who buys his own house and mortgages it is merely pay-
ing rent to the mortgagee out of his income, just as an
ordinary tenant pays rent to his landlord. Seeing that
the ordinary income-taxable person is taxed on his rent,
which has no more claim to exemption than his wine-bijl,
furniture outlay, grocery account, or any other item in
his expenditure, the Ham only knows where the mort-
gaged shareholder’s grievance comes in. An income
remains the same old income whether you possess mort-
gaged house property or no property at all, the drivel of
a legislative land-agent notwithstanding.
• • ♦ •
At a conference of stock inspectors, recently
held in Queensland, it was stated that Australian pastora-
Hats lose £300,030 a year through the careless branding
of hides alone. The brand, as a rule, is put on the most
valuable part of the hide, thus rendering it absolutely
useless for many purposes. There is no reason for this
except the pastoralist’s foolishness, unthrift and general
incapacity. Considering how this class ascribes all its
woes to the of Labor, it is interesting to know
that one of its biggest losses arises through its own in-
ability to put a hot iron on the cheapest end of a bullock.

A Melb. Chamber of Commerce deputation
lately interviewed the Commissioner of Customs re a pro-
secution in connection with the gun-branding frauds, with
indefinite results. If the deputationists were anxious in the
matter, what would be easier than to engage a detective
to find out a buyer who had been swindled, and then sail
in, on a criminal information? Certainly their blatant
honesty would not be so extensively advertised, but the
desired result would be attained, w’hioh, under present
conditions, is very doubtful.

After a period of suspicious stillness Mt. Drys-
dale “ booms ” again. Everywhere it is announced that
a new formation, heavy in pyrites and rich in gold, has
been found, and the mine is again declared to be “as
rich as ever.” Shares, which rapidly receded to less than
£5OO, hive gone up, and cheap advice not to sell at any
price is given. Not a word concerning the treacherous
character of the formation, the ill-defined ore-body, and
the fact that it is impossible to estimate what the mine
will yield, as, like most N.S.W. mines, it has been gouged
a lot.

The directors of the Ernest Giles W.A. gold
expedition are already issuing signals of distress to the
shareholders. After encountering great difficulties in
forming the 00., they say they have met with nothing hut
delay and obstruction from quarters which should have
given them the most loyal support. After being advised
that Mr. Giles had left Southern Cross for Coolgardie, they
found out that “he refused to proceed without an addi-
tional large sum of money was placed to his credit; but
as purchases had been made at exorbitant prices, your
directors, considering that the money already' remitted
had been spent in an extravagant manner, thought it
advisable to send Mr. A. H. Barclay to W.A, to thoroughly
investigate matters and to start the expedition without
further delay.”
* * * *
Dawson, one of the Londonderry men, is a well-
seasoned prospector who has put in a little time on most
of the early diggings of N.S.W. He was the first to work
beach-sand on the Richmond. Dawson knows the value
of secrecy in a gold-find. He cleaned his copper-plates
every night when working the sand, and was left alone
till he had worked out the best of it, making for a time
£5O a week. He kept the Londonderry quiet for eight
weeks, at the end of which period his party had over
£20,000 of gold in their tents.

Tiger Inglis, writes The Bulieitn’s London
correspondent, delivered a most effective address on the
mineral resources of N.S. Wales, at the Imperial Insti-
tute, to a very good audience. He was loudly applauded.
With great and commendable self-restraint he did not
trot out too prominently his King Charles’s head—the
Cremorne coal-mine under Port Jackson. “Tiger”
is living at present with his brother Colonel
Inglis, of Bygnds Hall, near Dunmow in Essex, where
is some of the finest shooting in England—and has been
obliged for sport to kill the familiar and partially tame
pheasant, in place of the more ferocious feline.

Competent mine-managers are still in strong
demand for Westralia. A London co. asked a Melb. firm
the other day to get it a manager for its Coolgardie
property ; and the only likely candidate—a man who
usually is glad to earn £250 a year—now demands £l5OO.
As a matter of fact, almost every man with any preten-
sions to competence in this direction has been picked up.
Two managers who were getting £7 10s. a week each
in Ballarat district a month ago, have just been trans-
lated to Coolgardie at £lOOO a vear apiece.

Editor Bulletin, —Re your par. of Deo. 22.
The only crushing of Ivanhoe stone was 80 tons, which
was crushed at the Otis mill here, during a period of eight
weeks ending July 28, and costing over £7 a ton for
crushing only. The yield was 3400 z. Since then no
crushing has been made, for the simple reason that there is
nothing to crush with, as the Otis mill is unavailable, and
the only battery nearer than Bayley’s, the Lake View, was
not started till last Oct. and can only crush its own stone.
—Yours, Brian Hookbr, A.R.S.M., Manager.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The legal manager of the New Victoria G.M.
Co., Coolgardie, tells us that the co. did not, as stated,
crush five tons for 18oz. 7dwt. per ton by battery, and
260 z. per ton by chlorination. The 'eault in question was
from two parcels of 2J tons each, or an average of 8ioz.
per ton. The five tons was roughly picked so thatthe
heavy cost of carriage to Victoria should not result in a
loss. It is considered that the hulk of the stone will go
3nz. per ton ; 20 tons, taken from all parts of the mine,
just treated at the Bendigo Coolgardi- Co.’s battery,
returned 57oz. retorted gold, which, when the newness of
the plates is considered, would fully equal 3oz. per
ton. The amount of water available for treating the 20
tons was very limited, and had to be used over and over
again.
Cabled that one Gorrie, speaking at a London
meeting of Londonderry shareholders, stated they
“ would be unable to exhaust their mine for a genera-
tion.” Seeing that the mine is not proved below two or
three score feet, Mr. Gorrie knows nothing about it. Tub
Bulletin knows nothing more ; but it has pleasure in call-
ing him an Ananias on general gold-mining principles.
“ It is only the pick that can see into the face.” Earl
Fingall, not to be beaten, asserted that the reef as being
worked would go 6000oz. to the ton, and 20ft. further
down 600oz. Very likely; and how many tons? The
secretary said over 480,000 shares had been applied for.
If the applications are bona-fide, and the shares are to be
paid to £1, this puts the syndicate’s profit beyond doubt.
Time now' to set to work and do something for the share-
holders. The golden hole, it is announced, is not to be
worked “ until the battery has been erected.” Consider-
ing that specimen stuff is never put through the battery,
and that hundreds in interest are being lost by allowing
the OOOOoz. stuff to be in the ground, it would appear that
the promoters are already openly treating their min# as a
market-concern.

The Empress of India and Scandinavian claims,
seven miles from Coolgardie, are said to have been pur-
chased by the Eldorado G.M. and P. syndicate, of Sydney,
and the purchase-money is said to lie the largest yet
paid by N.S.W. for any W.A. mine—which is an indefinite
sort of recommendation, for the amount is unstated.
This appears to be the first time these claims and the pur-
chasing syndicate were publicly heard of on this side.
It is news, too, to learn that “bulk samples, taken from
various parts of the mine, have given a result of 113oz.
gold per ton, while it is estimated that the whole faces
will yield, at least, 8 to lOoz. by ordinary battery
returns.”

circumstances.

Parke and Lacey, of Sydney, are turning out a
neat little battery of three 2501b. heads capable of crush-
ing 20 tons a week, running 120 with a six-inch drop.
Some miners who have one near Barraba, N.S.W., speak
of it as a marvel of cheapness and efficienc}', and with it
legitimate miners are freed from their miserable depend-
ence upon company-promoters and capitalists. These are
great recommendations.

Tom Slattery says he learned two cham-
pagne-drinking wrinkles from “Old Jack” Robert-
son—one, never ice your wine; the other, always
drink it out of a dry glass.

the wealthiest man in the N.S.W. Legislature, is

Rev. Theophilus Parr, now presi-
dent of N.S.W. Primitive Methodists,
preached his first sermon when nine years
of age. His other achievements are that
he introduced the first printing-press to
West Africa, and compiled a more or less
valuable dictionary.
Writing about the death of a distin-
guished member of the Australian Anglican
Church, the eldest son thus publicly deli-
vered himself : —“ lam sure you will sym-
pathise with me —with us —the only true
Church of God. He lies dead in an oak
coffin in the drawing-room ‘ like a warrior
taking his rest.’ But look beyond ! Can
you not see the two gleaming countenances ?
That of Jesus when He sees him enter, and
that of him when he sees our Saviour 1”
A Bulletin correspondent has had
an opportunity of seeing a proof of the
reply to the Westminster Gazette articles
on “the Mahatma roar,” which Mrs,
Besant wrote upon her arrival in India. In
it she frankly says, “On the letters I was
duped,” and adds, “I willingly take any
bl une for my gullibility that may be cast on
me, for I wish only that the facts may be
known.” She sums up by saying : “With
regard to the future, I had hoped that Mr.
Judge would have resigned the vice-presi-
dency on the issue of the enquiry. As he
has not done so, I think he should be officially
requested to resign by the sections. But
if he refuses, and if he cannot be deprived,
lam not going to resign from the Theo-
sophical Society because one cannot remove
an official, elected before these objections
to him arose. Were he now elected pre-
sident, I should resign, because that would
imply approval by the society of his course
of action.”
The late David Berry, the Coolangatta
(N.S.W.) millionaire, never, it is said, bought
any land without first tasting it literally.
Probably the worst case of “ earth hunger ” on
record.
Years ago, at an up-country function, the
local Mayor said genially to old Jack Robertson,
the guest of the evening : “Now, I suppose, sir,
it is my duty to find you a partner for the first
quadrille.” “You be damned,” snuffled the old
man, “ as far as women are concerned, I believe
in survey before selection.”
Every man should try to live so that the
world won’t bo made very much better by his
death.

Octogenarian White (father of R. H. D.
White, M.L.C. of N.S. Wales), drowned in the
Wairarapa, was universally known in pastoral
circles as a crack station-manager, especially on
the Darling, up to 30 years ago. At Bathurst, he
was once disgusted with an honorary magistrate,
who appeared on the bench with a black eye to
deal with the drunks. Remarks by White led to
a hostile me ting, with pistols. The amateur
beak, in a very shickery condition, fired first.
White then refused to fire, saying, “ Man, you’re
too drunk !”
Dear Bulletin, —You ask “ Is Anthony
Hope related to Gov. Hopetoun ?” Very likely.
His “Half a Hero” is an Australian story in
which much of the action passes in what may be
taken for Melb. Government House—though the
plot would better fit Maoriland. The leading char-
acter—Medland—is thus suggestively described by
a reviewer:—
He is a middle-aged man and an Australian Labor-
leader who has had a history, for a lady with whom he
lived for many years was not his wife, hut somebody
else’s. He has a daughter who is pretty, etc.
Now, mark that “etc ” ! —P.M.
The three Premiers of N.S. Wales, S.A.,
and Westralia were, as distinguished visitors,
given seats on the floor of Vic. Assembly. The
view of these three skulls and that of Premier
Turner, from the Press gallery, revealed a sur-
prising cranial similarity. The four Premiers
have heads of about the same size and shape—
Reid’s being, if anything, slightly fatter than the
others’—and they are all bald to about the same
extent. Reid’s “Giotto’s O” face is in strange
contrast to that of his brother, Hugh R. Reid,
chairman of Melb. Chamber of Commerce, who is
a little, sharp-featured, vermilion-skinned, scarlet-
bearded geranium.
Now that Lord is married some of
our San Francisco belles may breathe easier and
thank Heaven that there is no possibility of
becoming a peeress this year. His lordship made
a poor start on his honeymoon. It is not good
form, unless in English society (that is, the
society which receives the Earl), to get boiling
drunk at your wedding breakfast. Even Ward
M’Allister, who assigns to the obedient New
Yorker his place in society, can hardly condone
this. That his lordship is a mighty poor hand
at sobering up is evinced by the fact that this
morning drunk assumed the proportions of what
is known in Americanese as a “holdover,” and
held over so long that the fair bride had to woo
her lonely pillow, while the noble groom was put
to bed. Those little eccentricities should not,
however, at all interfere with the desire of our
girls to elevate themselves in society and get
properly wedged on to some sort of nobility.
If I were a young woman I’d rather marry an
English bank-clerk, who banged his hair, than
be the bride of the proudest effete noble who ever
“ slewed ” to a kettledrum, or beat a hotel-keeper
out of a week’s board.—S.F. Newsletter.

Mrs. EDOUN BRYER , “Forty Years' Ex-
perience of the Australian Stage.”
One Australian Governor who has the
best and most expensive set of false teeth in the
country, whenever his heart warms to a new
acquaintance, takes them out of his mouth and
explains their construction at great length, and
tells where he got them, and how much he paid
for them, and urges his friend to get his false
teeth a,t the same establishment. Persons of
weak digestion don’t like that Governor much.
Lady Kintore, as the world knows, long
ago gave S. A. Chief Justice Way a Pomeranian
pup. The latest development of his Honor’s craze
in regard to this lordly animal is that his Supreme
Court associate carries it under his arm whenever
a distinguished visitor has to be seen away from
the Adelaide railway-station.

The Late JOSEPH CLARKE.
A certain Australian Supreme Court Judge
runs a jam factory in the locality of his country
residence, and during vacation may be seen in a
big white apron, stirring up the boiling pulp with
a ladle.
People ask : What’s the meaning of Alfred
Bennett, who has just sold out and retired from
Sydney E. News, being interviewed by a Sydney
Daily Post reporter ? Is Alfred going into har-
ness again ?
The N.S.W. Governor and the N.S.W.
Premier are Scotchmen; the Cardinal and the
Chief Justice, Irishmen ; and the Primate and
Admiral, Englishmen. Bub where is the noble
Australian ?
Judge Boucaut, of Adelaide, whose wife
was recently left =£50,000, is constantly haunted
by the dread of poverty. If he only had 3000
shillings a year, instead of over that number
of pounds, he could not live more frugally.
The death of Lady Hanson, in
Adelaide, during January, recalled a society
episode long forgotten. Her husband, who
died in March, ’76, was Chief Justice of
S.A., but the upper ten turned their backs
on him because he married his housekeeper.
Henry Lucy, in S.M. Herald, gives
credit to Cecil Rhodes for taking care of
dead Lobengula’s three sons. The incident
is not exceptional. Greek brigands regard
it as a sacred duty to provide for the
orphans of people who have been murdered
in the way of business.
Taverner, Yic. Minister of Agricul-
ture, tells this yarn. Some enthusiastic
students of the art of perfume-making at
Dunolly Govt. Scent .Farm sent the Minister
a sample bottle for trial. Taverner care-
fully stowed it away in his trunk at the
hotel, but the cork being loose the scent
escaped. When the waiter was ordered by
the Minister to pack his trunk the odour
was very prevalent. On meeting Taverner
he said, apologetically: “Sorry, sir, you
left your trunk open last night, for the cat
must have got into it.”
No man could be as great a statesman
as J. B. Patterson looks. Since he has
been a knight he has more and more culti-
vated the magnificent air, and always speaks
with one leg slightly bent and thrust for-
ward, one hand on the hip, one finger wag-
gling, and one cuff arranged over the hand
—just as British statesmen pose in the
illustrated papers. The contrast with Premier
Turner is most marked. The latter is
shabby in dress, wears a common bowler
hat, rides on a threepenny tram, and hasn’t
as much “side ” as a flounder.
Mr. Justice Dodds (Tas.) had a painful
experience lately. Whilst walking down a quiet
Hobart street swinging his cane he was so unfor-
tunate as to strike a small but vigorous dog, who
retaliated by hopelessly rending the trousers of
Justice.
The story goes that a certain Australian
Governor, while “out on his own” one evening,
was introduced to the smartest of Sydney barmaids
as “Doctor Something.” “ Glad to meet you,
Doctor,” chirruped Hebe; “ you look awfully like
old Gov. , only, of course, much younger and
handsomer. Seltzer or soda, Doctor?”
Geo. Gordon M‘Crae’s novel, “The Man
in the Iron Mask,” will shortly be issued. Two
or three publishers have been confessedly timid
about its “improbabilities;” for, although the
scene is laid in the time of Queen Anne, the
plot begins and ends in Australia. The author
managed to get hold of some old records of the
time of Defoe, which gave him a good start for a
thrilling tale.
Randolph Churchill’s meteoric career
dazzled chiefly because meteors are miracles in
the Conservative sky. In any other quarter of
the heavens he would never have risen so high to
fall so far. At least half his celebrity comes from
the environriient in which he found himself when
he to make it. Yet Randolph Churchill
was no brilliant shallowpate. He said true things
as well as smart things; and his campaigns against
the ponderous dullness of Tory philistines were
ably planned as well as cleverly conducted.
When, after five years’ guerrilla warfare, he
became Secretary for India, and a year later rose
to be Chancellor of the Exchequer and leader of
the House of Commons, he displayed a reserve of
strength for which few had given him credit. Bub
Randolph Churchill could not “stay,” and when
he left office after a few months’ trial his political
career was definitely closed. Since then he has
been travelling in search of the health ruined
in the stress of political combat, and his
re-appearance two years ago in tiie Parlia-
mentary arena was utterly devoid of interest.
Now he is dead at 46 ; having (so it is said)
fallen a victim to the morphia habit, which he
contracted when in anxious search of a cure for
his overstrained nerves. His short, eager, intense
life is a monument of fin-de siecle conditions, with
universal neurosis paving the way for the Ragnarcik
of civilisition.
The late Joseph Clarke, herewith repre-
sented as he was ere the flowing bowl filled him
jut and Mrs. Clara Parker took his hair off, leaves
(jo enemies to enlarge upon his little failings. Of
“Joe” Clarke, as of his brother William, Bart.,
it must be said that the vast fortune left him by a
penurious father enriched a very accessible sort of
charity. Generosity comes easy to rich men only
when they are that way disposed. Joseph Clarke
was so free with his inherited coin as to justify a
belief that he had a bit of gold in his heart to
begin with. What he gave in public was a small
matter compared with the amount of his private
benefactions, wise and otherwise. He was always a
friend to the widowand thefatherless, and if hemade
a large distinction in favor of saucy little widows
who required setting up in the hotel line —well,
there’s nothing in the New Testament against that.
He became, under pressure from bad advisers, a
most unholy show in the Clara Parker scandal,
but behind the evidence of folly for which he paid
so dearly, in more than a financial sense, lay the
fact that Joe Clarke’s pocket suffered a tremen-
dous amount of punishment before he could be
induced to kick. From a high political and social
point of view this man was unadmirable. His
existence, as a large landholder and self-indulging
idler, was a regrettable circumstance. But his
death is sincerely regretted by many people who,
having served Joe Clarke, or been served by him,
wish that all capitalists were as courteous and
openhanded as our bacchanalian brother gone
before.

H. R. ROBERTS, Maggie Moore’s Leading;
Man.
Besides this, almost every steamer has had depu-
tations to the captain about the food. Returning
stone-broke fossickers aren’t over-particular, but
the steerage-fare on most of these boats is simply
disgraceful. On board the steamer the writer
went over in, meals for 300 were cooked in a little
caboose hardly big enough to hold the cook ; so
that the mullock-heap of meat-scraps intended’for
stew had to be left outside—to leeward of all the
tobacco-spitters and a reeking stench coming from
the defective sanitary arrangements. And over
this a monkey capered. There was this stew
three times a day. Once a chicken-bone, picked
clean of all meat, was found in it—proof that
the saloon plates were scraped into the steer-
age stew-pot. This bone was hung up in the cabin
as a warning. “Duff, ho ! ’’—then plates were
scraped of grease and meat into the middle of the
table to clean them for the “ duff.” But the worst
was the bread. It was stacked in an open locker,
flush with the deck, in a cabin where 25 men were
more or less sea-sick. The very stowaways work-
ing their passages were given joints with the crew,
while passengers, who had paid, were treated like
hogs. The writer had one decent meal—when he
and two others made a raid on the saloon galley at
midnight and stole two sheeps’-heads.
GOOD NEWS FROM ACROSS THE SEA,
We must all agree that last year’s financial crisis
was about the worst our country could have gone
through without breaking under it. Employment
and money have unfortunately got scarcer whilst
the cost of living remains the same. One man
however, at the Antipodes has thought of us*
Mr. 1 rossard, the eminent head of the Swiss Cigar
factory, with a keen foresight set to work to
supply his old Australian friends with a really
good cigar at a cheap price. This result has been
attained by adopting a cylindrical shape, which
is the cheapest to work, and in reality the most
suitable for any cigar. It was the original old
shape of the early cigar-rnaking period. Fros-
sard s Oavour Cigar is a mild, very aromatic, and
wonderfully cheap smoke ; packets of eight are
sold for one shilling, and though appearance is at
first against it—being rough and not gracefully
pointed like other cigars—it will be found that in
quality it is far superior to other articles cost-
ing three or four tunes as much.

“ IMITATION
is the
SINCE REST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week passes but what the press exposes the bare-
faced and shameless practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all, they seem to thrive, and
the paper which hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and misrepresentation. They are scien-
tific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to their
value can bo found in every district, and such
being the case it is unnecessary to further extol
their virtues.

The same old disease. The Reid Govern-
ment proposes to build a court-house at Warded,
a village with a population of 426 persons, who
have hitherto been successfully fined 2s. 6d. at the
expensive Government premises at Ballina, only
10 miles distant. As another item of retrench-
ment, it is related that when Garrard was
made Minister of Ignorance in the Reid
Cabinet, he promptly had the telephone laid on
to his house at Balmain, a number of posts
being erected and a long line of wire stretched for
the purpose. A week or two later Jacob removed
to a more aristocratic suburb, and a good many
pounds’ worth of telephone is now attached to an
empty house.
In Victoria, the qualification
of the common juror is being a
ratepayer assessed at not less than
£2O per annum. Now, as the
working man’s rent has fallen to
not more than 7s. per week, no
laborer will be on a jury; it will
consist of salesmen, clerks, shop-
keepers, and small property-
owners—classes peculiarly liable
to accept as sheer gospel the sum-
ming-up of a judge, and also gifted
with an exaggerated respect for
Law’n’order.
J. C. Neild has been ap-
pointed to a seat on N.S.W. Civil
Service Commission. There is
only one conceivable excuse for
the appointment—that Neild, by
his defeat at last N.S.W. general
election, was deprived of his poli-
tical income. The fact that he is
only known to the public as em-
phatically a professional party
politician ought to have operated
as a bar to his appointment to a
position for which partisanstiip of
any kind is, or should be, an abso-
lute disqualification. Another
and still more serious bar would
be Neild’s long and close connec-
tion with the politico-sectarian
Orange institution. No matter
how high a man’s reputation for
personal integrity may be, par-
tisanship must warp his judgment;
and surely it is not claimed by his
patrons, the Reid Govt., that he
possesses the com manding intellect
which alone might be thought to
compensate for want of fairness.
Ifc is to be hoped that advantage will not
be taken of N.S.W. Minister Carruthers’zeai for
Labor settlements to saddle the country with a
heavy bill for land-resumption. Surely there is
enough public territory available for the purpose.
Minister Young, of N.S.W., complains
that rumors have been circulated to the effect that
the ,£216,000 extra which the Reid Govt, pro-
poses to spend on roads (and to charge to its
predecessors) isn’t “really available.” The money,
Mr. Young points out, was voted in ’93, and
therefore there can’t be the slightest doubt as to
its availability. Which is remarkable logic, to
say the least. Everybody knows the money was
voted, but as there is no such sum on hand the
ayailableness isn’t apparent. Treas. Reid should
sit down in a cool spot and ask himself how
money that doesn’t exist can be made available
by a Parliamentary vote or by any other means.

and duties of Victorian Speaker too exacting,
arranged with Chairman Mason that they should
pool their respective salaries and take half each,
on the understanding that Mason did the all-night
sittings.

A large, enthusiastic and representative
audience didn't fill Melt. Town Hall (as alleged
by an 1, e, and r press) at last week’s Federation
meeting. The night was hot, and the building
was not crowded in any part. The south gallery,
for instance, contained only 13 attentive listeners.

Oracularly remarks Sydney E. News :
We have heard men undergoing peculiar experiences
through a mother-in-law, but never before heard that
having a father-in-law was a qualification for a seat in the
Legislature.
Nonsense ! Had Honniker Heaton never had a
father-in-law, he would not now be representing
the Evening News in the British House of
Commons.
A weak old person named Melville rose in
Vic. Legislative Council to point out that the
Income-tax would “crush all thrift.” Just now a
man naturally wants an income and does his best,
by thrift or otherwise, to get one; but when the
Government takes 6d. in the <£ of his annual
revenue and only leaves him a miserable 19s. 6d.
to himself, he will be so much broken up that he
will try to avoid the ruinous impost by not having
any income at all. Notwithstanding which, Mel-
ville, M.L.C., will still have all the income he can
get and wish that he had a lot more, and every-
body else will do the same. Thrift is a tough
virtue.
Duncan Gillies, who was smuggled into
the billet of Victorian Agent-General at a salary
of .£2500 a year, will have to suffer a reduction of
..£lOOO from July Ist next. It is feared that little
Dunoan will not oblige his bleeding country by
being too much offended to stay on at >£lsoo a
year, but the drop in his finances is bound to hurt
the gentleman’s puffy conceit. There was no
real question of a breach of faith with Gillies in
this salary matter, for the appointment was made
by Patterson, behind Victoria’s back, and the
country subsequently washed its hands of Patter-
son and all his works. But the breach of faith in
Gillies is undeniable, therefore the retrenched
Agent-General feels at the present moment very
like a tin god who has been badly dented by the
heel of an ignorant democracy.

Editor Bulletin, —A Sydney tradesman
having a contract for some work “up the line,”
sent one of his regular workmen with a note to
the Labor Bureau, saying the bearer was hard up,
but he could give him a little work at an inland
town it the department would give the man a free
pass—which was given. This employer was boy-
cotted some months ago for cutting wages to the
bone. Always on the cheap !—W.T.
A recent World unearths an odd thing
about Gladstone. In 1850 he was very thick with
Lord Lincoln, whose lady bolted to Italy with a
certain Hon. Walpole. Gladstone obliged his
lordship by acting as a kind of private detective.
He tracked the pair, under assumed names, to the
Villa Manoini, Como. However, she was so full
of nous that he could only get a rapid glimpse as
she skipped into a carriage. Gladstone gave this
evidence in the divorce suit before the Peers.

A lately-returned Church
dignitary, in the course of a
pastoral visitation, once spent
a night at an up-oountry
station. One of his fellow-
guests was notoriously fond of
whisky, while the other habi-
tually limited himself i<> a
nightly glass, but that evening,
to the surprise and alarm of
their entertainer, not only the
bishop, but the whole party
passed the bottle in gloomy
silence, declining even to
moisten their lips, Cater on,
the host sat racking his brains
in solitude as to the cause of
this phenomenon, until realis-
ing the awful sufferings of his
thirstier friend, he took the
bottle and glass and stole on
tip-toe to hjs bedside. He was
warmly welcomed and given
to understand that his visitor's
abstinence had been inspired
by a fear oif going too far
before his lordship. Passing
on to the next room, the
squatter was again received
with acclamation, and the
confession that crony No. 2
had denied himself his drink,
so as not to start off the
weaker brother. Emboldened
by Jthese experiences the host
now ventured to tap gently at
his reverence's door, armed
with another “ nightcap.” In
this case, he found the stum-
bling-block had been a matter
of principle. In fact, it would
not have been seemly for an
ecclesiastic to indulge in the
presence ,of a totally-abstain-
ing laity. After this, the good
Samaritan banished all fears
that his house-party might be
translated, before mornings
into a fiery ohariot, and hav-
ing clinked glasses with the
bishop he went to bed with a
light heart.

The painful anxiety of Sydney Trades Hall
people to secure Gov. Duff for their opening ban-
quet, and the open disgust with which his excuse
for non-attendance was received, show how much
snobbery still lies embedded in trades-union
officials. The snub was richly deserved, and The
Bulletin hopes it will be repeated every time the
unions invite it. What have Australian workers
to do with an imported figurehead, and why should
alleged democrats yearn for the presence of
royalty’s representative at a typical workers’
function ? There is nothing in common between
Democracy and Guvmentouse, and, if Democracy
doesn’t know it, all the better that Guvmentouse
does.
» • *
The “victim” in the Sydney military
seduction case says (as per the newspaper-reports)
that, while engaged to marry a Civil Service clerk,
she succumbed six hours after first meeting the
go'd-laced hero ! The magnetism of Mars is pro-
verbial ; but, in the case of a Venus of 2G springs
this Barkis-like “ willin’’’-ness leaves little room
for the conventional suggestions of trustful inno-
cence and base betrayal. A fortress which has
been accumulating defences for 26 years does not
surrender in six hours without a suspicion of
treason on the part of the garrison. And there
was, it seems, no promise of marriage—the sur-
render was unconditional. Even excitable Major-
General Hutton, who, in his eagerness to make
Bayards sans reproche of the members of his tin-
pot army, is said, before the enquiry, to have sug-
gested to Gov. Duff the cashiering of the guilty
officer, now commences to see his error. “ The
woman that deliberates is lost,” wrote Addison.
And also the woman that doesn't, apparently. In
truth Addison knew more about wine than woman,
or he would never have uttered the showy half-truth.
Pope’s sweeping generalisation shows aCuter
vision : “ Every woman is at heart a rake.’’
* * •
At Gov. Kintore’s farewell procession from
Adelaide railway-station, a stout and protuberant
dowager, wife of a wealthy local merchant, made
frantic efforts to shake hands with the departing
lord. His Ex. knew nothing of her struggles,
however —mainly through the heroic defence of a
foot policeman who highly amused the crowd by
his resistance of the lady’s blandishments. It was
this lady, by the way, who was the central figure
in an amusing yarn which Lady Kintore has
doubtless long ago retailed to her English friends.
# • •
Neither Major Sebright nor Capt. Milner,
who were on Lord Kintore’s staff, would remain
to ease Mr. Way’s term of office as S. A. Lieut. -
Governor, and he has to fall back on Dr. Penne-
father, a lecturer at Adelaide University, who
was once private secretary to Gen. Jervois. It is
not likely that Mr. Way’s reign will extend
much beyond the end of next April. Already the
aggregate length of his service as Acting Governor
exceeds two years and three months.

The seven Rational Dress women, who went
to Fernshaw (Vio.) for their Xmas holiday in
knickerbockers, wore skirts in the train. They
shunted just before reaching Healesville, where
they emerged with legs, and with brown-paper
parcels under their arms, to the unbounded
astonishment of the railway men and Healesville
at large.

A Londoner married a Melb. girl at ’Ome
some three months ago, and came out here on the
honeymoon. Latter too long, perhaps. Was per-
mitted by his wife to run down to Frankston for a
day’s sea-fishing. Hubby, as a matter of fact,
spent his day somewhere in Lonsdale-street, and,
on his return, presented his astonished Australian
wife (as the result of his day’s sea-fishing) with
three Murray-cod weighing about 7lb. apiece!!
The temperature in that once-happy household is
between Bdeg. and 20deg. below zero just now.
# * *
Editor Bulletin, —Let me tell you of the
Gentleman of the Gaol Van. _ Circular Quay,
Sydney, 4.50 p.m. Nemesis, in the shape of
“ Black Maria,” awaiting her load. The water-
police range themselves to form a passage. No
word is spoken. The doors of the van are thrown
open. Men and women file out of their respective
compartments. A young man, with an old felt
hat pulled down over his face, evidently not
relishing the gaze of the curious. An “ old
geyser of a woman ” with wrinkled and bruised
face, dirty, repulsive, and feeble—so feeble that it
is with much difficulty she attempts to negotiate
the three steps to the ground. As she hangs
on tremulously to the hand-rail the officers
tender no assistance, but stand and silently gaze.
The young man, noticing the old woman in her
difficulty, turns toward her. His left_ hand
involuntarily raises his old hat as with his right he
hands down the battered wreck with the ease and
grace of a courtier. Just the act of a few
seconds, and then with bowed head he fol-
lows onboard the steamer. The whisk of a driver s
whip, the turn of a steamer’s screw, and the great
world moves on as before; but the Recording
Angel has put another dot opposite the word
“Gentleman’’—the Gentleman of the Gaol Van.
—W.S.

Certain zealous Sydney policemen insist
on “ running their hands over ’ persons who
emerge from the private entrances of pubs, on
Sunday nights, and it has happened that Sally
and Tottie, and women and girls of the family,
and lady-visitors have, on emerging, been
jammed up against the corner of the -house by
one “ trap,” while another searched their panniers
and corsets in pursuit of the pint of rum or flask
of whisky which wasn’t there.
# * *
“By love serve one another” is the text
on Salvation Army Headquarters, Melb., which
building was purchased at a sacrifice from the
mortgagees who foreclosed on the holders, the
Y.M.C. A. A helping hand to their brother
Christians could not be tendered by the Army
when such fi bargain offered. Passers-by read the
text, recollect the incident, and smile.
* * *
Phenomenal luck. MacPhairson is a
gilded squatter, and is now a widower for the
third time. His first wife died on a far-out
station and was buried there in a home-made
coffin ; his second died on a voyage to Maoriland
and was buried at sea ; the third was drowned
crossing a flooded river—the horse got out, but she
has never been seen since. So Fate has dodged
the undertaker’s bill for him every time.

To be the wife of the medical chief of a
public health department entails certain grave
responsibilities. At least, a Southern lady
occupying the position seems to think so. The
other day, she was about to make some purchases
in a large drapery establishment, when the slave
about to serve her coughed. “ Don’t attempt to
serve me!” exclaimed the dame, “that cough is
catching,” and she insisted upon being attended
by a menial with a clean bill of health.

At the first meeting of the Onehunga
(M L ) Borough Council, after the election at
which Mrs. Mayor Yates was ejected, a coun-
cillor, who arrived late, is said to have fallen, in his
haste, over a dark bundle on the stair. lie hur-
ri©dly struck a match, and found that the bundle
was the ex-she-Mayor listening, with an agonised
expression, at the keyhole. The present writer
has often been at Onehunga, and, if he remembers
aright, there is no stair at the council-chamber.
But that is a mere detail.

Mr. Alf. Greaoy, who has been appointed an officer on
H M V.S. Nelson, from the Tin-tack Office, where he ful-
filled clerical duties for two or three years, promises to be
one of the “ pets of high society.” Mr. Greacy is good-
looking, and at a recent “at home, ' given at the Naval
Depot in Williamstown, he created a most favorable
impression among the ladies. Since then he has been
JiHed on all sides, and his quiet, genial manner, together
•with a rather attractive appearance, has made him most
popular in society circles.
As the lines are not marked “advt.,” it is safe to
assume that the pretty Mr. Greacy did not pay
for their insertion, and that Brodzky aforesaid is
wholly responsible for the loathsome remarks. Is
it in accordance with the laws of compensation
that when the new woman takes to knickers the
bewchus sassiety youth should have his features
slobbered over in print ? The very next thing, no
doubt, will be : “Mr. Alf. Greacy, looking sweetly
pretty in a lovely confection of lavender pants
with a corsage of grey doth, was the cynosure of

The writer sent a cable to London Evening
News, stating that Stoddart had acknowledged in
the last test-match that the Australians showed
superiority in fielding, bowling, and batting.
Next day one of the Australian cable syndicates
received a message from London (and it was pub-
lished in big type with large headings) announcing
that the News expressed the opinion that the
Australians showed superiority in every depart-
ment of the game. Seems a waste of money,
doesn’t it ?

Dear Bulletin,— Many of our residents
have “ gone home ” (from Hannan’s) of late, and
there is now a boom in coffin making, grave-
digging and other things incidental to the plant-
ing” of the late-lamented. Owing to the scarcity
of alluvial gold, the gaunt and dusty dryblower
does not always know where to get his daily tinned
dog, so gravedigging is locally looked upon as a
light and pleasant means of earning an honest
shilling. So much so that two parties run a hot
and furious opposition, and are wont each to keep
at least three ready-dug graves in stock. And as
there is no properly - defined burying-place at
Hannan’s, party feeling runs high whenever
either of the rivals is favored with the care
of the deceased. Last week a poor fellow shot
himself. Both sets of gravediggers turned up to
secure the remains, each chanting the praises of
its own particular location. One firm quoted
£3 as the price of a grave; the other offered to do
it in style for £2 10s. This last was almost
accepted, when a brawny German forced his way
in and offered to do all such job lots at .£1 each.
His offer was closed with on the spot; but when
the Progress Committee inspected his site, it was
found that the wily Teuton had pegged out a
50ft. claim right on some played-out workings.
He had improved the shining hour by levelling
down the old tailings-heaps, leaving the diggers’
shafts open, and was therefore in a position to
undercut his rivals. To the credit of the com-
mittee, they decided not to encourage foreign
labor.—J.P. Hannan’s, 13/1/95.

alone, and scared the office-

Coolgardie boasts of bub one hall or
theatre, and there the public dances are held.
Coolgardie has now naturally reached the clique
stage—the bankers, merchants, and big mine-
owners, &c., don’t like tripping in the same set as
the soaped and shiny digger. Also, the wives of
the elite lift their skirts and clutch smelling-salts
whenever the mazy waltz brings them within
staring-distance of any one of those persons who
keep “ cool-drink shops.” After a recent concert
a social luminary—whose army drawl and padded
figure arc features of cultivated Coolgardie society
—stayed with his wife to the ensuing ball; but
was horrified to find himself swinging to the
strains of “ Dysey Bell,” with the girl who waited
on him at the tdble-d’hole, while his wife hopped
in the vulgar clutch of a local pug., the proud
possessor of a stoushed nose. “ Bai jove ! what
would my wife’s mother, Lady Boornburst, think
if she could only have seen her daughter partnered
to a low fellah who, some vffigah fellah said, ‘ had
battled a willin’ go last week ’ for a tenner ! He
actually told my waife, while dancing, that he
broke his nose falling over specimens. They
offered to * chuck him out ’ if I would stay—
howwid, wasn’t it ?’’

Dear Bulletin, —Within a hundred yards
of a largely-patronised hospital in Coolgardie lie
the festering remains of the recently-abolished
slaughter-yards; in the balmy summer evening,
when the western breeze comes sighing in over the
heaps of half-burnt offal, bones, &0., the convales-
cenf\come out of their tents and inhale draughts
of unvdulterated typhoid. The Corporation has
kindly allowed to be dumped in the same locality
a hundred tons or so of seething “rubbish and,
to make the surroundings still more cheerful and
appropriate, the hospital is situated close to the
track leading to the cemetery, whither are
harried daily with dismal regularity the scarce-
cold victims of Western fever and lack of medical
comforts, to be subsequently thrown into a scratch
hole. An incoherent mummery of words from a
perspiring parson, and back rattles the cart for
another beaten bread-winner, for whom some sad-
eyed wife and babes wait vainly in the East.
—Dryblower.

Caretakers, paying no rent, occupy houses
in Melbourne on the understanding that they are
to look out for tenants. One, who has let several
houses in this way, and who was probably
aggrieved at having to shift so often, recently
asked the owner for his “removal expenses.”

of riding 300 miles through rough Gippsland country in ten days. The notable feature of
the performance is that the fair equestrienne wore rational dress and was astride the horse

A summary of the benefits of civilisation
may be found in the box of a returned Kanaka.
It usually pans out cane-knives, tomahawks, axes,
murderous-looking bill-hook«, forbidden liquors,
and occasionally (a tribute to superstition) a
“ Glory-be-to-God ” book.

The “ unemployed ” at Shea’s Creek,
Sydney—who only get a week’s work in every five
when the weather allows them to work : if it
doesn’t it is their loss—finish their week’s labor
on Tuesdays but have to wait till the following
Friday to get paid.

Broken Hill, though in N.S.W., will take
its time, under the Standard Time Act, from Ade-
laide, and not from Sydney.

Dear Bulletin, —An experience of cheap
undertakers. A poor woman, whose husband had
died, called upon an undertaker who advertised
funerals at .£5. On asking what the <£s included
she was told “ a hearse, coach, coffin, clergyman,
and everything.” After being shown a photo, of
what a grand funeral it would bo with an extra
coach, she arranged to have the extra coach for
•£6 10s. in all. On the day fixed for the burial
the undertaker arrived an hour late with a ram-
shackle hearse and two dilapidated coaches, all
drawn by bay horses. After handing round 12
cards for the occupants of the two coaches, which
combined were only fit to properly accom-
modate eight persons, a start was made for
the cemetery. Arrived at the grave, there was
no clergyman, but the resourceful undertaker
borrowed one from an adjoining funeral, “giving
him the office” to cut things short. When the
burial was over, it was found that the hearse and
one of the mourning-coaches had set out to funeral
another corpse. This left the mourners nothing
to do but endeavour to crowd into the remaining
coach. On arrival at the first tram-line on the
road home the driver drew up and dealt out tram-
tickets to those inside, remarking that he had
driven them to the cemetery, but was damned if
he would drive them home; and as the tram was
about 50 times as comfortable as the coach, no-
thing much was said. Next morning brought the
widow a bill for .=£9 10s., the extra <£3 being for
cemetery expenses and damages to the coach, the
side of which came out through overcrowding.
Inquiries prove the undertaker’s whole stock-in-
trade to consist of what he had out that day.—
Paul Blunt.

Afc the recent test-match in Adelaide, just
when there was a lull, two tame dudes, dressed
up to date, were seated in a dog-cart behind a
smart-looking coachman. “Can’t you move on,
coachman? ” said the proprietor dude. “ I can’t,
sir—this doctor’s trap is in the way.” “ Well,
my deah man, you weally must—ah—ask him to
back out a little.” The doctor’s trap backed out,
the dudes’ coachman flicked his horse, the horse
jumped, and one dude flew out on to a fruit-stall
and tried to hatch some peaches, wl ilst the other,
a stout and healthy 14-stoner, just missed an
afternoon tea-party by six inches. Didn’t the
crowd roar!
The law is a conundrum. A Redfern
(Sydney) defendant, charged with having com-
mitted a nuisance by accumulating sheepskins on
his premises, was acquitted without a stain, on the
ground that the municipal by-law only compelled
him to keep his premises from causing a nuisance ;
while the prosecution admitted that the nuisance
was caused, not by the premises, but by the
sheepskins on the premises ! Daniel might come
to judgment for a million years without getting
level with Sydney stipendiaries.

Sydney Women’s Literary Club propose
discussing Zola at an early date. The members
have been reading him carefully, and the general
opinion is that he is very naughty—but, oh, so
nice!

Down South, the other day, a Chinaman
was charged with having committed an indecent
assault. It was the old story : Little girl enticed
by lollies, &0., &c. The father of the plaintiff
vehemently denied having attempted to square
the case for a consideration of one pound, but sub-
sequently admitted he might do so for Jive.

Gem from Answers (London, of course) :
A Cornish Nonconformist minister named Hall, while
on a visit to Tasmania, one day lighted upon auriferous
strata of a peculiar kind, and, in a nonchalant way, made
an offer to the Government for the land. The land was
formally sold to Hall for 1300. It yielded 5000 ounces to
the ton. One-twentieth share was sold to D’Arcy, of
Grosvenor Square, who draws £40,000 a month from the
investment.
Such is the history of Mount Morgan !

The term “Crickster,” now coming into
free use in Sydney as descriptive of persons of
certain qualities, originated in an attempt to ob-
sequiously gratify the elect of West Macquarie.
# * *
The swagman will get acclimatised in Aus-
tralia by-and-bye. A while ago a North Queens-
land digger got married to a girl of 22 or
thereabouts, and the very day of their marriage
news came of a promising rush in the Batavia
River district, so the pair started out manfully for
their honeymoon to walk the 250 miles that lay
between them and the scene of action. The
present writer hasn’t heard of them since, but
trusts the bride arrived there safely, and that they
met with luck. More recently a N.S.W. man
with a wife, two small children, an infant, and a
heavy swag, marched from Albury to Corowa
(N.S.W.), and thence to Broadford (Vic.) in’
search of work. Part of the road lay
over swamps where he had to carry the
family, one at a time, through long stretches of
water two feet deep. His progress was regulated
by the walking abilities of the wife and the two
children, his average rate being five miles a day.
There was no work to be found at the end of the
long journey, so the family walked on again and
disappeared over the horizon, the man carrying
the swag, the wife burdened with the infant, and
the two small children trotting alongside. Pro-
bably the whole family is still walking. Almost
the only regular work to be obtained in Australia
now consists of walking.

My Dear Moorabinda—
“Sowing the Wind,” at Sydney Lyceum, is
what people call a “ costume play.” The dressing
in the new comedy, which had the honor of re-
introducing the unspeakably-popular B and B.
co. to Sydney amid howls of welcome, belongs to
the days when George the Fourth was king, or
thereabouts. I cannot say that the bird’s-eye ap-
pearance of the ladies in the play fills me with a
wil-d longing to throw back on the sartorial trail.
The good old days were evidently very bad old
days for women who weren’t pooket-Venuses, so
to speak.
Mrs. Brough was the only stage-lady who
looked anything but a sketch in the up-and-down
clothing of the period. On her first appearance—
when the applause was almost too enthusiastic for
her self-possession, I thought (there is a point
when prolonged applause becomes painful, though
first-night audiences never seem to feel it)—Mrs.
Brough wore a mouse-colored travelling-dress,
concealed by a brown velvet cloak, and topped off
by a brown velvet hat, presenting a most artistic
whole, very different (intentionally so, I have no
doubt) from Miss Romer’s appearance in sandals, a
shawl, and a coal-scuttle bonnet, or from pretty
Grace Noble’s, who had curls and a pancake hat,
and a square dress with little bows up the front,
which a jin-de-sibcle doll would jibe at. I thought
Miss Noble’s giddy giggle threw quite a new light
on our great-grand mothers, by the way. If the
majority of the back-number of our she-ancastors
were as unpromisingly flighty in manner as Miss
Fretwell, I don’t see how anybody can feel alto-
gether confident on the subject of the family-tree.
I didn’t approve of Cecil Ward making his first
appearance as the confiding hero in a grasshopper-
green suit. .Rosamond was as good as gold, of
course ; but still, green is a fatal color for a youth
to wear, whose sweetheart likes to talk about her
past in a haggard tone of voice—even if it turns
out to have been her mother’s past, after all. The
men, by the way, have the best of the dressing in
“ Sowing the Wind.” Titheradge, who looks like
the village priest in the last act, is a tone-poem in
snuff-color, to begin with ; and Mr. Dorrington,
as Sir Richard Cursitor, wears a pretty-colored
topcoat, that is both chronologically correct and
strictly up-to-date, as the long-skirted coat with a
high waist is one of the latest horsey-fashions from
’Ome.
But I must confess I cannot quite see why the
dramatist made an old-time setting for “Sowing
the Wind.” Up-to-date tin-de-siecle problems
sound incongruous in the mouth of a woman who
curtsies with profound and graceful humility to
all the men and tabby women in the play. Every
artistic curve of Mrs. Brough’s backbone ex-
presses the woman who venerates men as monarchs
of all they survey—but once she assumes a per-
pendicular attitude her sentiments brace up too
apparently, and no Green Carnation of them all
could hope to improve upon the succinct roll-call of
naked and embarrassing truisms which Rosamond
lets fly at the bald-head of the good old gentleman
whom she “ made a leg ” to with so much sweet-
ness and deference in Act I. The graceful
curtsey, by the way, is one of the pleasantest
things in this interesting play. Women should
revive the curtsey, for it suits ’em. There is
something in it so suggestive of a oat coiling for a
spring.
Things must be coming to a most uncharitable
pass in Sydney when husbands are forced to
advertise in the daily papers that their honor is
intact —as one of our wealthiesfmen saw fit to do,
the other day. That healthy, philosophical gag,
“ They say—what say they ?—let’em say,” must
be falling into desuetude when such a public
notice-extraordinary as that gets into one’s morn-
ing paper. But it only shows what tattlesome
ones there are and what a long way ahead (or
behind) of the mote and beam business we’ve got.
I believe Sydney has rather a strong reputation
for piping-hot on dits. I vividly recollect an
Englishwoman asking me once, in a scared voice,
if all the ladies in Sydney were *** ? She put it in
asterisks, and so must I, and really she may have
meant s raphim, for all I actually know. She had
undergone a course of afternoon-teas, though,
where the women had been engaged in capping
stories about their friends, so I didn’t suppose she
questioned me as to whether local belledom wore
wings under its gigot sleeves or not.
But—sharp though the slander may be—it is far
from worldly-wise, in my opinion, to make a
“public notice ” matter of it, whatever form the
gossip may happen to take. Stories are always
more satisfactorily lived down than hounded
down, unless you can catch the original mischief-
maker and pin her up to discourage the others.
But complete innocence has been its own champion
from time immemorial, and if the Paulina Piys say,
for instance, that Darby has gone off with Flossie
Footlights, or that Joan has fled with Don Juan,
what completer defence could you have than the
appearance of Darby and Joan aforesaid eheek-
by-jowl in some public place ? Nobody is obliged
to believe all that he or she hears, but seeing’s
quite a different matter.
There is one thing that Australians can lay
claim to —and that is being the best-washed people
on the surface of this grubby earth. In France,
very broadly speaking, they only wash about once
a month—being mostly direct descendants (in the
matter of ablutions) from that King Louis whose
morning rinse was a drop of spirits of wine in
either palm; and in some other European coun-
tries about once a year, if the weather’s propitious;
while the Englishman who hurls his “tub” at
you in fiction, by all independent accounts knows
less about it in absolute fact than you’d imagine.
In most British places of entertainment for man
and beast they charge you 2s. for a hot bath, and
Is. for a cold one. In hundreds of “hotels” and
more or less fashionable lodging-houses it is still
the custom to use the antique “ tub,” and up to
very recently a bath-room was the exception
rather than the rule in the British household.
Somebody in Sydney was taxing a Johnny-come-
lately with the absence of baths on his native
mud-bank the other day. “Oh, but you know,”
be protested, “ nearly all the modern houses have
bathrooms.” “ Ah—that must be since the Aus-
tralians began going ’Ome,” said a still small
voice.
In Australia the- great majority is clean. Be-
side the domiciliary bathroom, the place is a net-
work of swimming-baths—Coilingwood (Vic.)
being the latest in the field with public baths to
cost .£l3OO, mostly raised by the Australian
Natives’ Association. Though I don’t think all
baths quite fill the cleansing bill—mind you.
There is one Victorian place of ablution where
the first bath is sixpence, but you pay much less
for the privilege of disporting in the second bath,
which consists of the more or less greasy water
left by the sixpenny aristocrats who have gone
before ! But still, a bath is a bath, whether you
are last in or not, and the well-crusted Tory jibe
about “the great unwashed” has little or no
significance in Australia.
I was at Newcastle (N.S.W.) the other day—the
coaly seaport where the degage unattire of the
populace on the ocean beach provoked ex-Attorney-
General Heydon’s famous edict re bathing-gowns
from “ neck to knee’’—and saw at a glance that
there was too much bathing among the women and
children. Shoals of anaemic bony infants swarm
on the sands and soak in the water with blue lips
and lifeless complexions—little, skinny, yellow
creatures, not one of whom realises one’s ideal
child with a round face delicately tinted (you
don’t look for apple-cheeks in these latitudes).
And not only little children, but growing girls are
perilously allowed to spend whole afternoons in
wet, flattering bathing garments, puddling about
on the sand, and soddening in the sea, in a way to
make Dr. Anna Potts turn in her grave (if she
has one). I never saw one sprightly, pink-skinned
woman, girl, or baby during the whole of my
stay in Newcastle. The surf has washed the
“ tuck ” out of them, and I thiuk it is quite time
somebody let them know it.
Mrs. Lees, Sydney’s scarcely-unpacked brand-
new Mayoress, holds her first civic reception at
Sydney Town Hall next Thursday, 7th Feb., at 3
p.m., and I only hope that, with a new mayoral
regime, a new style of civic welcome will obtain.
I mean, it is quite time the official hand-shake —
flabby, nerveless, meaningless, recalling the tail of
a dead schnapper or a cold shoulder of mutton —
was cut off in its old age, and something else sub-
stituted—any tiling, for a change, short of rubbing
noses, to which the teetotal party might strongly
object, in defence of their principles.
A smile and a bow is really the correct way to
receive a mob of strange people, whom you don’t
know from Adam and Eve, and who don’t know
you either, and would do away with the spectacle
of the hostess holding out a soiled and uninviting
glove for “ middling ” late-oomera to shake, if they
feel like it.
This system of indiscriminate paw-snatching
always seems to me to be a most remarkable phase
of “society.” In one’s plain dealings with one[s
fellow-man and woman even an introduction is
received with a mere inclination of the head —but
let the public loose on the premises and lo ! each
comer must be pinched by the hand as if to deter-
mine whether he or she is real or not! At the
same time, though, there are some people who
actually enjoy pawing the multitude. Only the
other day I saw a city councillor walking his
horse over a street-crossing (to set a good example,
I suppose) and when ho caught the admiring eye
of a traffic-director in the road, he leant out of
his trap and shook the poor man’s unwilling hand
as if it had been a doormat! But I thought it a
distinct waste of patronage, under the circum-
stances. It’s only when you’re breaking a bye-
law that this sort of cheap familiarity comes in.
In seeking out a culchawed sort of wind-mill to
tilt ac, a Melbourne “society” paper complains bit-
terly in the elegant language of the parlor-boarder
against “the custom of entirely disregarding in-
vitations or replying to them out of season—a cus-
tom that is eating away at the small heart of cul-
ture Australia possessess.” Of course that applies
to Melbourne, but I suppose maimers are the
same in Sydney, are they not ? If they are there’s
balm in Gilead and milk in the cocoa-nut. What
I mean is that “our” vice-royalties are past-
masters at the same game, and I am always most
willing and eager to believe that even the bad
manners which emanate from Govmentouso are
strictly Prince of Walesy.
Only the other day the secretary of a fashion-
able club sent an urgent note to the vice-regal
p.c., and, although it was an invitation for his Ex.
to go and gambol somewhere or other with the
club, no answer turned up. Too late for apologies,
the club secretary met the vice-regal pen-man and
taxed him with his neglect. “ Beg pardon, ’m
sure,” he responded, without turning a hair, “ but
Ai really forgot it, dontcherknow.” Melbourne
people who do likewise can at least point a con-
fident forefinger at Sydney Govmdntouse in ex-
tenuation of their social faults.
You’d imagine that, of all on earth, it
would be necessary for the ballet-dancer to keep
strictly sober while at work. But I was told the
other day of a not undistinguished example of the
strict contrary. She was the possessor of an
ineffably dainty figure, and always kept exquisite
time, never blundering a step, but she was usually
drunk as Chloe from her first entrance. A pecu-
liarity of hers (in Australia, at least) was that no
matter how “ elevated ” she might be before going
on, directly she got her music-cue and whirled on
to the stage she was instantly as sober as an owl.
The excitement took the place of an electric
shower-bath, I suppose. But, once in her dressing-
room, her potations got the upper hand again—
which explains quite a lot of amusing stories.
Two promising new musical compositions, issued
by W. H. Paling and Co., are the “Irene”
gavotte, dedicated to her Excellency of Banana-
land, and composed by W. A. Caflisch ; and
“Jack’s Toast,” a song by F. Burry. The
“ Irene ” gavotte is one of those dainty pizzicato
bits which remind the appreciative uncultured of
a cat crossing a wet pateh, and the gifted com-
poser has thoughtfully added fingering so a% to
prevent enthusiasts who don’t know from trying
to play it with their clenched fists, I presume.
“Jack’s Toast ” is a baritone sea-song, naturally
introducing the ladies, who are Jack’s toast, in
point of fact. After a careful hearing of the
chanty, one must come to the regrettable conclu-
sion that “Jack’s Toast and Water.” would have
made a more suitable title.
Talking of music, that clever and indefatigable
Australian composer, Maude Fitz-Stubbs, whose
“Heather” and “Orlando” waltzes have been
bought by a big London music-publisher, and
played by Dan Godfrey’s band, is leaving Sydney
for London, per Rome, next March. Miss Fitz-
Stubbs takes a number of new and clever compo-
sitions with her, wherewith to set Father Thames
on fire.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Sydney Telegraph begins to make" rude
remarks about the feeble and wobblesome Premier
Reid, and to speak kindly at times of its old
enemy, -Dibbs. In a recent par. it points
out that President Diaz is one of the greatest of
living statesmen, and then calls him the Dibbs of
Mexico, by way of emphasis. Something has
gone wrong with the works.
Ed. Bulletin, —Premier Turner’s biz.
could not be affected by the “landed gentry.”
The police-court branch under Sam. Lyons could
not be impaired, nor could the other, as the clients
are drawn from tradesmen, publicans and shop-
keepers. The firm is that of a middle-class plod-
ding attorney, worked up well by the Vic. Premier,
who uses the local friendly societies for all they
are worth as advts.—G.
The Sydney schooner Elsa, trading to the
South Seas, recently found at Vani Kori, the
scene of the wreck of the vessel which bore_ the
lost French explorer, La Perouse, some silver
coins, dated 1785, and a squarely-cut stone, which
looked like a tombstone, and bore an indistinct
inscription. These relics would be very precious
in France, where the mysterious fate of the dis-
tinguished navigator has taken strong hold of the
popular imagination.
Sydney Daily Telegraph last Tuesday
had a whoop about the N.S.W. sugar industry
If it were not for the present duty of £5 per ton sugar
oould be imported and sold at, say, £l2 per ton, instead
of £2l as at present. It is estimated that the Austra-
lasian production during the coming season will exceed
the consumption of 160,000 tons by fully 10,060 tons. The
importations from Mauritius reach 14,600 tons, and from
Java 40,000 tons per annum ; so that the prospect of a
glut of sugar and a large unexpertable surplus seems in-
evitable, which, coupled with the fact that the world’s mar-
kets are all in a similar condition, and are prepared to
supply us at half-price, leaves but one reasonable conclusion
as to thefate of our own insignificant industry. The amount
of duty paid last year for imported sugar was £544,233.
The total value of all the sugar yown in the.colony during
the same period is roughly put fit about £300,000, which
makes it appear as if the public were subscribing annually
about one-third of a million of money for the privilege of
owning a few acres of sugarcane, and a quarter of a mil-
lion for providing dividends for one monopolistic refining
company. With a free port for sugar at the present
moment, the finest sugar could be procured at about one-
half of what is now paid by retailers. In the English
counties the artisan can purchase his pound of sugar at
one penny. We, at the source of production, are asked
an advance of 150 per cent.
Now, in tiie first place, N.S. Wales last year paid*
not .£544,000 in duty on imported sugar, but only
.£144,000, so that there is here a wild discrepancy
of just <£400,000 which helps materially to make
up a case where none exists. Secondly, sugar was
quoted in Saturday’s Telegraph as low as .£l9
per ton, so that £2l i's the maximum price.
Third y, £5 duty, added to .£l2, makes £l7
not .£2l —in any case. Fourthly, if N.S.
Wales, as the Telegraph asserts, will this
season supply itself and have “ a glut and a large
unexportable surplus,” then it won’t pay any duty,
so that the wail about the alleged £544,000 paid
under the tariff is hopeless bosh, for the “colony ”
will straightway cease to pay anything. Also, the
glut will inevitably reduce prices, so that the wail
about the over-taxed consumer is also bosh.
Fifthly, the sugar that the English artisan gets
for Id. per lb. and that which costs 2£d. per lb.
in N.S. Wales are altogether different brands.
Sixthly, the last available Daily Chronicle
quotes sugar in London at .£l3 ss. to .£l6 per ton,
and as the freight and incidental charges, as
shown by the Telegraph, are over .£5 per ton, it
would appear that the finest protected Queensland
sugar, at .£2l, is cheaper than the finest imported
article would be even without any duty. A few
other discrepancies are held over until the next
time the D.T. makes a fool of itself on this topic.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
In England, M‘Laren being an amateur is
“Mister." With the Sydney orbwd he ia “ Archie.” In
one of-the matches on the Sydney ground, MSLaren was
fielding near the fence, and a Sky hit came towards him.
“ Alias it, Archie,” yelled one'of the crowd, “Mias it,
Archie, and vou cun kiss my sister.”
•‘* * *
Moses’ name being Jewish, many of the crowd
think the man is, too. Thus in Melbourne, when Moses
was going to make,a catch in the a Melbourne
barracker shouted, “Oh, Lord, it’s all up with Melbourne
—the ball’s going into the blooming synagogue 1”
* • * *
Crowds are partisans. When in Melbourne the
Vies, are in and a Sydney bowler is bowling on the off-
tiieory, the crowd, fearful that their batsmen will lash
out and get caught, keep up a fusillade from the fence.
“Bowl straight!” “Can’t you see the wicket?”
“ You’re a cock-eyed howler.” “Can’t hit a house if
you’re inside it,” &e., &o. But when the Sydney men are
in and the Vies, are playing “ off ” and failing to tempt
the wary batsmen, then comes a change of fire from the
ground crowd. “ Hit the ball !’’ “ Lay on.the wood like
Bruce does ! ” “ Why the devil don’t you try to score? ”
“ Isn’t it big enough? Would you like a pumpkin to hit
at?” &c., &c.

contest on Adelaide Oval on the Saturday after the last
test-match. A. E. Trott, too, playing against the Melb.
Club, failed to get a wicket, though more than 60 runs
were scored off him.

Over £5OO Ims been collected for Geo. Giffen in
