,A. E. Trott says the Englishmen lost the third
test-match chiefly through over-carefulness.
* * * *
The ( barracking Worrall received from the
Sydney crowdiwas not half as severe as that w-hich Lyons
had in Melbourne. Lyons let Iredale do a lot of his
fielding for him, and the many runs which resulled from
Lyons’ defectiveness may alone exclude him from the next
two test-matches.
* * * *
No selection committee oould allege against
Bathurst M'Kibbin what was pleaded against Iredale—
that he was physically unfit for the hurly-burly of an
English tour. M'Kibben rode 40 miles on Thursday,
travelled all night in the train, and paralysed the Vic-
torians on Friday.
« • ♦ *
Cricket cannot require the “staying power”
essential in other mamly games, else Garrett and Black-
ball), members of the first Australian Eleven which visited
England 17years ago, and who played for their respective
provinces on Saturday last, would long ago have been laid
on the shelf.
* * * *
In the N.S.W. —Yic. interprovincial match the
Vic. eleven reached only 181 in their fisrt innings, the
small score being greatly due to M’Kibhen, w ho took five
of their wickets for 36 runs. Several of the Victorians say
that M'Kibben’s fast ball is a decided throw.
Some 7000 people, on Anniversary Day, went to
Sydney Cricket Ground, and spent most of their time in
objurgating the drizzling rain and bitter wind. Not until
3 o’clock did play begin, and it closed before 5. Iredale
batted with splendid vigor, sending Ids score of 28 up to
86, and wdien his cuts and drives sped over the surface of
the field, and cut a thin, green track through the rain-
beaded grass, the crowd grew warm with enthusiasm.
But the ball, however, spent about half its time in the
sawdust, and while A. E. Trott or C. M'Leod would be
giving it a dry coat the crowd unmercifully barracked
Worrall. Worrall is reputed to have derided N.S.W.
players by stating that the Australian Eleven could be
formed without them. The Sydney crowd resented
this by deciding Worrall. Thus, wdien the firso hall bowled
was driven by Iredale »o the fence, fifty voices shouted :
“ How do you like t-hat, Worrall ?” “ Put Worrall on ! He
can play 1” “ Let Worrall bowl!” and so on during the
bare two hours of play. Moses was bow led for 1 by a
straight ball, wfilch deceived hinfin lire flight.
• • * •
Critics say that one or two of Iredale’s graceful
forward strokes would betray him most sadly on English
wickets. But they also say that the crack would soon
drop down to the peculiarities of the English grounds.
* * * ♦
Graham is studying dentistry, and spent con-
siderable money in securing a professional library when
last in England.
•*• , »
At one time it was thought that Gregory would
not have the heart for great things. But the fact is the
little man is all heart. Who can forget the day, in
England, when he scored 50 against Richardson, in an
All-England match, and got so badly knocked about the
legs by the fast bowler that he could not field afterwards ?
• * ♦ ♦
Donnan’s first innings of 40 in the match against
Victoria was made by sound, clean cricket. Mentioning
Dontian recalls the'insigniticant difference which occurred
in Melbourne, when this season's first match against Vic-
toria was played there. One day, at the close of play, as
umpires and players came in together, Donnon said to
Flynn, the Vic. umpire : “ You gave two decisions against
us.” And Flynn retorted : “ Yon ought to he umpire,
Donnan." Thus the quarrel began and ended. This is
what one Melb. paper raised’so frantic a hullabaloo about,
and what a Sydney daily went into hysterics over.
• * * *
Iredale has never bought a bat; all his bats have
come to him by presentation. Most of the time on Satur-
day he used a bat which was brought to him from Eng-
land by Alec. Bannerman. That savant selecti dit in the
shop of its maker, Wisden. With this bat, Iredale com-
menced this season’s play in the Sydney match against
the Englishmen on Nov. 23. He scored, in his first (linings
it, 133, and up to date has made off it a total of 613.
Last Saturday the w r et and heavy ball started the pegs
that had been driven into its face lo bind it, and
Iredale, anxious to preserve his trusty weapon for the
next test-match, came to the members’ pavilion and
changed it for another by the same maker. He made 13
runs with this new bat, which was presented to him by an
admirer, and was bought from the firm of Jaa. Martin and
Co., of Clarence-street, Sydney. Graham, when in Eng-
land, made 1242 runs with a Wisden bat, and Wisden
offered him the pick of his establishment. Graham
accepted the offer and chose what he considered the best
two or three.
• * * •
At one stage of the game, last Saturday, the
N.S.W. scoring-board read 2 for 102 ; Iredale, not out, 81;
later on it read 3 for 151; Iredale, out, 86. Iredale went
in first and made the pace.
* » * *
Perhaps the player who wears the heaviest pads
is Turner. On Anniversary Day Donnan put these pads
on by mistake, and, after running in them for a little
while, came back to the members’ pavilion and changed
them for a lighter pair. Iredale and Gregory prefer
skeleton pads.

It is probable that Richarffson, on his native
heath, will be found as dangerous to the next Australian
Eleven as he was to the last one that went “home,” in
which case the difficulty of providing against the differ-
ence between English and Australian cricket conditions
will he generally understood. A supply of fast Australian
bowlers will not meet it, unless they can have slow
wickets to work upon— i.e., it would not much benefit
Australian batsmen to become accustomed to play howl-
ing similar to Richardson’s under circumstances which
make it much easier than in England. His very moderate
success here offers little or no encouragement to culti-
vate fast bowders, anyway. They don’t pay, for local
purposes. If we did cultivate them, and sent two of the
best specimens to England with a future team, they would
be less troublesome to the other side on average dull
wickets than the Britisher’s “ lightning deliveries” would
be to the Australians. Put the case this way. Sfoddart’s
team make only about 30 per cent, more runs, on an
average, against our men on hard Australian ground, than
they could score off the same bowling on a typical English
wicket, where Qiffen’s team woußl stand an excellent
chance of getting out for a 50 per cent, smaller total than
they are good for in their own country. If the two sides
are evenly matched here, the change to English con-
ditions, with Richaidson as an important item in the
calculation, must; in the present waiter’s ©pinion, dis-
count the Australians’ chance. Catapult bowling, with a
little bit of brains in it, will always stick up the Austra-
lians on the particular class of wicket—sound and true,
but slow—which they so seldom get in Australia.
» • • *
Gov. Duff’s son may nob have the seat of a
Hales or a Kelso when finishing, but he gets there all the
same. On Saturday, he fairly out-generalled the amateurs
when he won on Towri. His three wins, on Towri and
Bodkin, are the more meritorious from the fact that some
of the best of local jockeys have been unable to win
when entrusted with mounts on the pair of speedy,
but uncertain brutes.
♦ * ♦ *
Carbine’s case recalls the wail that went up
when Barnum deprived England of the elephayit Jumbo.
The parallel may hold good to the last. Jumbo, as if he
wanted to cling to the soil from which the D.T. corres-
pondents cried he should not move, was only got on board
with difficulty. Now, judging by his antics before his
great races, there would be trouble in getting Master Jack
to step on to a ship.
* * * *
Ganmain, engaged in the Newmarket at Bst., is
whispered about as a real “ cert.” He is a stable-com-
panion to Roseleaf, who won the Tooronga Handicap, at
Caulfield, by five lengths, carrying Bst. 71bs. in Imin.
15jsee. Roseleaf has ost. 61bs. in the Newmarket, and, as
Ganmain is said to be her equal at level weights, the
Newmarket is all over it rumor has much truth in it.
♦ • * ♦
How is it that jumping horses last so much
longer than flat racers, and that in defiance of the terrible
knocking-about their legs receive, their heavy and
fr quent falls, and the infinitely more trying contests
over longer distances than* their brothers of the “ legiti-
mate game " have to undertake?
♦ * • •
The Derby winner, Harvester, is generally con-
sidered-the best-treated horse in the Australian Cup. As,
however, he did not run in Melb. Cup, no proper com-
parison of liis chance with older horses can be made. It
must'be remembered that Malachite, the Derby runner-
up, finished third-last in Melb. Cup with the feather of
6st. 121bs. up. On the whole, it is probable that the
three-year-olds this season are nothing out of the common.
Punters must not forget’that Carbine, as a three-year-old,
could not win the Australian Cup, in which he carried
exactly the same weight as Harvester has this year.

bad backing “ when well.’

Lillie Bridge (Sydney) proprietors might well
give trotting just one more chance. They have shown
conclusively that they will not stand any nonsense from
the trotting-horss people ; and the writer has it on good
authority that a trot for a fak stake would be rushed by
the owners, who are tired of inaction, and not likely to
resort to their old tricks—for a time at least.
♦ * * *
Adelaide bookmakers naw act as tote commis-
sion agents, depositing the money received in the machine
and paying out the divs. received less Hperoent.com.
Israel Moses, however, retains all over 10 to 1; if
the machine pays £2O for every 10s. the bookie osly pays
out 10 to 1, thus clearing £l5 on the transaction. This is
a form of robbery made possible by the stupidity of the
authorities who refuse to run a ss. totaiisator, and conse-
quently the public on the flat, who bet in half-crowns and
five “ bobs.” are forced to wager through the “ commis-
sion agent” and submit to a double commission, in all 11A
per cent. Some bookmakers, under this new system, are
making more money ihan they did in the old days, and
take no risk whatever.

f From Pearson's Weekly (London), Deo. 1
Bicycle riders in Southland, Australia, are reciuired to
dismount iwenty-two yards from an approaching horse,
and draw their machine past.” Our contemporary is
slightly incorrect. The now historical case against the
champion bicyclist, Henry Parkes, at Christchurch (Q.),
where Sir George Qiflen, the presiding judge, inflicted a
hue of £5O for dismounting at a distance of 21 yards,
caused such an uproar that the obnoxious law was re-
pealed in the S.A. Legislature, mainly through the efforts
of Premier The Right Hon. Lord A. E. Trott, P.O.

If electorate football is played next season in
Sydney, the successful Randwick club will be entirely
extinguished. Among all the players that have con-
tributed for the past two or three seasons to the Rand-
wick club s brilliant exploits, not one lives at Randwick,
* * * *
Jhe fleet of Brisbane racing-boats that came to
Sydney for regatta time did not at first see much that
was beautiful about Sydney Harbor. Driving gales, and
grey-green seas, and dismal pouring skies, forbade the
hoisting of a sail for 10 days, or so. The Brisbane 22-
footers go in for a centre-board 2ft. long by 6ft. deep;
whereas moat of the Sydney boats have centre-boards
ranging from sft. to 10ft. long, with a sft. drop.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The national game of football having badly
infected Maoriland police force, the Commissioner has
ukased that no leave will be granted to play in matches,
and also that policemen are not to join clubs, as the
practice interferes with discipline and duty.

Blackham much admires Howell's bowling, but
Giffen and Lyons (who are bowlers) don’t. Blackham
intends to watch Howell closely during the interpro-
vincial.
* ♦ * ♦
The so-called ■“ umpire’s boat” at the Sydney
sailing-race on Saturday week was a snare and a delusion.
Hundreds paid extra fare, on the assumption that the
umpire was on board, and that, therefore, a good view of
the race could be got from the steamer. When the
crowded packet pu,t off, it was discovered that the umpire
was comfortably housed on a small launch.
* * ♦ *
The 22-feet boats interprovinoial championship
at Sydney Anniversary Regatta produced a great contest
between the Queenslander, Bulletin, and the Sydneyite,
Irex. There was a spanking southerly, and Irex led Bui-
lerin round Manly buoy by 12secs., hut once on a wind the
Bananalander showed her superiority, and at the Flag-
ship had limn. 31sees. to the good. Running to Fort
Denison Irex picked up lost ground, but it was the Bulle-
tin's day out and she ultimately finished 21secs. ahead of
her chief rival.
♦ * * *
North Shore Rowing Club won the senior fours,
at the Anniversary Regatta, for the hifherto fatal fourth
•year in succession. Sydney Club won three years in suc-
cession, then Mercantile took a hand and won for three
years ; followed by three wins for the North Shores. The
superstitiously-inclined thought the latter’s time had
come on Saturday, but they won for the fourth time in
succession, and broke the spell. ,
The success of the new Cannalin remedies
for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-stre'et, Sydney.
N.S. W. Minister for Works Young is
attacking the double-billet system in the right
place at the top of the tree. 'Hitherto Harley
(engineer for harbors and rivers), Hickson (en-
gineer for roads and bridges), and Vernon (Govt,
architect), have been also members of the Board
of Water Supply and Sewerage. Mr. Young pro-
poses to retire Vernon and Hickson from the
Board, make Harley chairman, and let Hickson
be engineer for harbors and rivers as well as for
his present department. Naturally the pluralists
don t like it j but they have wisely decided to grin
and bear.
Railway Commissioner Eddy, writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, has to
some extent been made a oatspaw of by a
band of people who are dissatisfied with the
management of the South - Eastern railway,
especially with regard to the war which has for so
long been waged with its rival, the London,
Chatham and Hover, and which arose prin-
cipally from the competition for the conti-
nental traMjp. Mr. Eddy seems to have allowed
himself to be the nominee of this clique. Sir
Edward Watkin having retired, everything points
to a better understanding between the two
companies. Undoubtedly Mr. Eddy’s manage-
ment of the N.S. Wales railways has enor-
mously enhanced his reputation as a railway man
with the English public, and he would have a
strong following if it came to a vote. But
railway shareholders are a conservative lot,
and are always disposed to support directors, who
are generally so heavily armed with proxies that
they can carry whatever they make a vital ques-
tion. Besides, radical changes are not approved
of in railway management, and, therefore, if Mr.
Eddy chose to submit himself to the ordeal of the
ballot, he would find a very solid vote against him.
His election, should he happen to be successful,
would undoubtedly lead to the resignation of Sir
Myles Fenton, who has long been the manager of
the co., and who, failing any swell shareholder as
a candidate, would naturally look to the chair-
manship as his legitimate promotion.

Mills, the Londonderry man, turned up in
Sydney last week, and was soon besieged by press-
interviewers and insurance-canvassers. He escaped to
Maoriland per mail. By the purchase between them
of Dawson’s share for £3OOO, Mills and Huxley came to
own half the mine. Mills is the third shareholder who
has married within the last two months, and Huxley
(who, like Mills, is level-headed and worth say £70,000) is
the only remaining one unmarried. Proposals may be ad-
dressed P. 0., Coolgardie.
♦ * ♦
A Melbourne suburban tobacconist has for
weeks past been doing a roaring business in imported
cigars, which he advertised as “Smuggled Cavitas at six
a shilling.” Although the goods had paid duty in the
ordinary way, the whole lot was seized by the Customs,
confiscated, and the tobacconist fined treble their value
under the Act which (perhaps very properly) treats a
vendor, who falsely' represents his wares as smuggled,
precisely the same as if the goods had actually cheated
the revenue.
Which reminds the writer that about six months
ago he bought a small keg of “ real, genuine Jamaica
from a mysterious stranger who had, so he said, got it
off his vessel in the Bay. The sample which the smuggler
then and there drew from the keg was undoubtedly At
stuff, and the five-gallon keg was bought for 16s. 6d. A
further thimbleful of rum and 4J gallons of water were all
that keg subsequently panned out, _ A tiny tube of rum
was tacked over the hole from which the ‘ smuggler
drew the rum, and the remaining vacuum was filled witn
water. The riling thing is that the “smuggler knows
that the unfortunate writer can’t squeal, as he had fully
intended to “do” the revenue. The “rum merchant
is now, no doubt, successfully playing the plugged-Dutch-
cheese racket at remunerative rates-viz., 3s. od. for flu.
of artistically-painted cement representing a round cheese
and a 40z.-phig of delicious cheese inserted in the hole
from whicli the buyer gets a tasting sample.

Mr. Rickarby, of Richmond, in a letter written
to a Melbourne daily paper, states it to be his opinion
that the great and long-felt want of Coolgardie w-goats.
Most people think it is gold, but that by the way. There
certainly seems to be enough unadulterated rock m Cool-
gardie to support any number of goals, When not re-
galing themselves on rock, the goats might dig for gold
with their horns. There is no reason why a Coolgardie
Wild-Goat Prospecting 00. should not be floated on the
London market, considering the kind of Coolgardie com-
panies which have been started in London during the last
few months. *

The S. African mining-boom in London is
caused by the demand for shares from Berlin and Pans.
The yields and divs. are unaltered, but the boom is beyond
precedent. »
There has been no jumping on or near the
Wealth of Nations. Some may see an explanation of this
in the list of owners.

A late Pall Mall publishes a portrait of
Leonard Jacobs, the negro who first found diamonds in
South Africa. Something like ten millions have been
made out of that find, but the discoverer is depicted as a
gaunt and hungry old man with hardly anything on save
a pair of patched pants.
» ♦ * *
Many Melb, registry-offices are now conducted
in a most shameless manner. Men are openly swindled
out of their fees, and billets are simply put up to auction
—the highest fee wins.

Editor Bulletin, —Melb. vegetable and fish
hawkers have to pay market-dues in each suburb operated.
For instance, a man buys his stuff in Melbourne, takes it
round Richmond (dues Is.), then Prahran (dues Is. again),
and if not sold out tries St. Hilda—another Is. Probably
in going home via South Melbourne with his empty cart,
the S.M. inspector will t ry a bluff for still another shilling.
—Dead Beat.
* * * *
When London becomes a desert and a waste
place, and a region where the pelican builds his nest, the
city region will make a reasonably good goldfield, and
claims pegged in Lombard-street and Leadenhall-street
will yield decent returns. It has been proved by careful
prospecting that the city mud has become auriferous
through long centuries of wear and tear of the coinage in
money-changing, and gold and silver are both present in
appreciable quantities. They can also be found in the
West End, but in the poorer regions of the East End the
most careful prospecting yielded not even a color. A
sovereign never remains there long enough to get worn
The Austin Gold-mines, Ltd., have purchased
the Evening Star claim adjoining their property on the
Island, Lake Austin, W.A. The amalgamation of these
claims, with a solitary autocrat at the head of affairs,
should turn out well for all concerned, as some of the
richest ore shoots found on the Island have been in the
Evening Star claim. In addition to the battery a 2000-ton
cyanide plant is to be erected to treat the tailings—as
well as to sift a large concrete formation going over half
an ounce per ton —after dry crushing in an Otis mill.*
The Golden Bar Gold-mining Company, Cool-
gardie, are making quite a departure from ordinary
mining practice by the method, recently adopted, of
treating their ores. They are now erecting a dry crush-
ing-plant (Bald mill)from which the ore will be treated in
Allan's amalgamating pans, and will thence pass into the
cyanide vats. The results will he looked for with con-
siderable interest. The plant is being erected by the Otis
Engineering Company, of M' Ibourne, the inventors of
the system.*

At the Forty-fourth Half-yearly Meeting of Shareholders, held at the Banking House, Queen street,
Brisbane, on Thursday, 24th January, at noon, the Hon. F. H. Hart, M.L.Q., Chairman of the Board
of Directors, presiding, the following Report and Balance-sheet were unanimously adopted :
HEAD OFFICE,
Brisbane, 16th January, 1895.
To the Shareholders of
The Queensland National Bank, Limit™.
The Directors now beg to submit their Forty-fifth Half-yearly Report, with the Balance-sheet duly
certified by the Auditors.
The Net Profits for the Half-year ended 31st December last, after making
provision for Rebate on Bills Current, Interest Accrued on Deposits, and
Bad Debts, amount to ... ...
Balance from previous Half-year ... ... ... ...
The Directors recommend that this sum should be appropriated as follows
To Dividend at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum on £4 per share
To Dividend Duty thereon
To Balance carried forward to next half-year ... ...
,£46,182 16 9
Under the Scheme of Arrangement the sum of £360,000 became payable to the Colonial Treasurer
in instalments extending to 30th September, 1895. Your Directors have to report that they completed
the payment of this amount on 3rd August last.
The total sum paid to the Government since May, 1893, in reduction of the Bank s debt, amounts
to £1,163,650, exclusive of interest.
Since the 30th of June last the Branches at Aramao, Georgetown, Isisford, Montalbion, Mutta-
burra, and Petrie Terrace have been closed.
In conformity with clauses 85b and 86 of the Articles of Association, Alexander Brand Webster,
Esq., now retires from the Board of Directors, but is eligible for re-election, and offers himself accord-
ingly. There is no other candidate. _ .
The Shareholders will be required to appoint two Auditors in the place of Francis Q. Southerden,
Esq., and W. Robertson Strong, Esq., who are both eligible for re-election, and to fix their remunera*
tion for the ensuing year.
By Order of the Board.
E. R. DRURY, General Manager.

IMPRISONED ON A SHIP.
The steamship Normannia, from Hamburg,
arrived in the port of New York on Saturday,
Sept. 3rd, 1892, with cases of cholera on board.
Many of the ship’s company had died on the pas-
sage. At Hamburg and elsewhere in Europe the
disease was raging. The authorities in America
were alarmed lest the scourge should be introduced
into that country. Hence they quarantined the
Normannia, with every soul of her passengers and
crew. The writer was a passenger. It was an
awful time. Death was among us and on all
sides of us. Nobody knew who next would fall.
We were imprisoned. Liberty never seemed so
fair, nor so far. We could neither fight nor fly.
There we were—hundreds of us— perfectly well,
and yet bound together as with chains, that the
health officer of the port might see whether the
plague would not yet break out in our midst.
When at last —after weeks of this —we were set on
shore, men lifted their hats and reverently said,
“ Thank God I ”
This was being shut up under conditions to
make it horrible and fearful. Yet, any form of
incarceration is bad enough. Here is a woman,
for example, who says, “ I never moved a yard
from my own doorstep for twenty weeks t ” Her own
house was a prison to her. Who had sentenced
her? A judge? No; a power greater and more
pitiless than any judge.
Her tale runs thus : In April, 1882, whilst living
at Lasher’s Farm, Old Sampford, Essex, a fire
broke out, and the family were burned out of
house and home. We have no call to remark on
such a calamity. The very thought of it is fit to
make one shiver with dread. For most of us it is
like the world coming to an end to experience such
a disaster.
Well, what happened after that the lady shall
tell in her own fashion—the best of all fashions,
because it is plain and straight to the point. She
says: “ Owing to our bedding being damp from
exposure, I took a bad cold, which brought on
rheumatic fever. For fourteen days I was con-
fined to my bed, and for twenty weeks I never
moved a yard from my own doorstep. After a
time the fever abated, leaving me weak, languid
and low. At first I had a sickening taste in the
mouth, and a poor appetite. No matter how
simple and light the food was, I was afraid to eat,
for it was sure to give me pain at the chest and
sides ; so I often had to loosen my corset and un-
dress myself during the day. I could not bear the
weight of my clothing.
“I was constantly spitting_up a sour, frothy
fluid, and had a gnawing pain at the pit of the
stomach —like hunger, and yet different. _ It was
with difficulty I voided the kidney secretion, and
my bowels, ankles and legs began to swell. I got
worse ; I was in agony night and day, and could
not put my foot on the ground. Soon afterwards,
a husky cough took me, and my throat filled with
a thick phlegm. I could not sleep, and was never
easy. Later on, I had often to sit up in bed, for I
felt as if I should choke.
“ Year after year I continued to suffer in this
way, growing worse and worse, until I despaired
• f ever being well again. But who can tell when
t .miil£ will come, or when relief? A wonderful
Providence is over all.
“One day in June, a book came by post de-
scribing Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup and what
it had done for many poor sufferers. I got a
bottle from Mr. Suckling, medicine dealer, and
after taking it for a short time all pain left me,
and I gradually gained strength. By taking an
occasional dose I have since kept in good health,
and can eat and digest any kind of food. (Signed)
Mrs. Lydia Green, Moor End, Great Sampford,
via Braintree, Essex, Aug. 24th, 1892.”
Now, in order that Mrs. Green’s clear and
truthful statement may be of use to others (as
she desires it to be), we must add a word or two.
The bad cold she caught at the fire no doubt
“brought on” the rheumatic fever (as she
relates), but there was something back of the
cold, for a cold never causes rheumatism. The
rheumatic seeds, or poison, must already lie in
the blood ; and that poison is always created by
pre-existing indigestion and dyspepsia, whether
the sufferer knows it or not. This is proved by
the fact that Mrs. Green’s chief ailment for ten
years after the fire was not rheumatism, but
indigestion and dyspepsia and dropsy, which is
one of its results and symptoms. When the
digestion was finally righted by the remedy she
alludes to, all her apparent maladies ceased
together. Why ? Because she had but one, as we
have said.
Ah, yes, Disease is a stern gaoler. And how
sweet (and cheap) is liberty, obtained by Mother
Seigel’s help.
A Maryboro’ (Q.) medico :—“ Cancer is
extremely common among cattle in this district.
Cancer is also more prevalent in this district
among human beings than in any other part of
Queensland.”
Messrs. Frost and Shipham,' of George-
streeb, Sydney, have acquired the Australian
rights of a new invention called the “ hygienic
ear-pad for the telephone.” Telephones are used
by all sorts of persons, many of them unhealthy.
Each person who uses the instrument presses the
pad to his ear, and leaves upon it the signs of his
presence in the shape of perspiration and microbes.
When a telephone-receiver has been in use for
some time analysis of the accumulated deposit
furnishes an interesting essay on disease and the
methods by which it is communicated. The
hygienic ear-pad, which fits on to the receiver, is
specially designed to meet this unwholesome diffi-
culty. It is composed of many layers pf white
material, so that whenever the topmost layer
shows signs of depreciation it can be torn off and
a clean surface obtained ; and it is prepared with
an antiseptic compound which prevents infection.
The pad also improves the efficiency of the instru-
ment by excluding outward noises, and thus
makes the telephone considerably more service-
able.
Hazell’s Annual for ’95 makes the tenth
issue of that popular work, and is a great im-
provement on its predecessors. It contains in a
condensed form the doings of the world for the
past year; biographies of men who have recently
come to the front by their own exertions, or been
shoved to the front by circumstances ; an account
of the year’s discoveries in all branches of know-
ledge ; and a general report of things that have
happened, and of things that should have hap-
pened, but didn’t. The new articles in the present
edition deal with anarchism, Arctic exploration,
astronomy, new baronets, knights, peers, C.M.G.’s,
and the like; new books, theology, Corea, the
China-Jap. war, divorce, geology, canals, bullet-
proof cuirasses, law, cholera, hypnotism, explora-
tion and several dozen other subjects. Also, it
serves the purpose of a small, condensed, bub
exceedingly complete, cyclopaedia of all modern
things, persons and circumstances, and_ all the
recent history of the world is to be found in its 716
pages. For its size, Hazkll’s Annual contains
more varied and up-to-date knowledge than any
other work The Bulletin knows of.
Looking over the list of winners on the
racecourses, on the cricket-field, and at all the
sports during the holidays, one is astonished to
find that all the successful men are smokers.
Still, you hear it said that smoking is injurious
and weakens the nerves; and those who think so
are quite correct to a certain extent. No doubt
heavy smoking of tobacco containing deleterious
compounds would weaken the nerves of a giant,
but consult a few of the gentlemen mentioned
above, and you will find that almost without
exception they are lovers of the one cigar of the
age, viz., Frossard’s Cavour cigars, which, being
mild and fragrant, are a treat to smoke. They
have all the pleasant flavor of the 'excellent leaf
from which they are prepared, without the un-
pleasant taste of the nicotine, which, having been
extracted in vacuo, leaves the smoker the essential
aromatic flavor of the tobacco. If you do nob
already smoke, then clo not hesitate, but try them
once, and you will smoke them always. Fros-
sard’s Cavour cigars are sold everywhere in
packets of eight for Is.

With other folk I’ll ferry you.

Dear Bulletin, —A tight-place. Away
back in ’69, Bob Williamson, carrier, and his
boy were riding from Gilberton to Townsville.
At the Broken River they camped for the night,
making their fire at the foot of a big gum-tree
which was partly hollow. Before midnight the
tree burnt through, falling on both, killing the
black boy, and pinning Williamson by the legs,
one of which was broken above the ankle. There
the carrier lay with the tree slowly burning
towards him until 11 a.m. next day, when some
teamsters came along and released him. By then,
the fire had come close enough to singe his
trousers and blister his legs and arms. William-
son'now lives at Windsor, Vic. —B. the 8., Nor-
man ton, Dec. 26, ’94.
Sydney Domain, Sunday. Young Snuffle-
buster loq.: “ Look at me, a brand plucked from
Sitan’s clutches—(a trifle is asked to assist the
good work)—Were, hi hask, was hi to be found a
year ago on the Board’s ’oly Day ? Wy, in the
fiush, a headin’ of ’em, a playin’ two-hup. A
throwin’ hup of my crowns, and a watchin’ ’em
cornin’ down. A tossin’ an’ a pitchin’ along o’
my wicked terbacker-chewin’ companyuns—
(Throw it in, friends ; its all in the Ma-aster’s
service. Glory-be-to God ! Another shillin’ !
Thank-YOU-sir.)—But were ham hi to be found
now of a Sunday ? Wy, a praisin’ the Board,
along with my dear young friends ’ere ” —(looking
round with an evil and unctuous grin at the ring
of fresh, young, hymn-book-holding maidens) —
“not in the bush, playin’ two hup, but ’ere, doin’
the Ma-aster’s work. Still •” “ Heading
them,” quickly interjected a vigorous-looking
hearer, pointing to the red banner, dotted with
silver coin, over which the orator kept a watchful
eye. Here, the evangelist seeming a little flabber-
gasted, the band struck up “ Waiting for the
Master,” and the obvious moral was lost amid the
braying of trumpets and the banging of drums.

Said the little red-bearded man with the
bandy legs : “Now, that’s a horse as you oidy get
the chance of buying, at a bargain, once in a life-
time.” The new-ohum looked at him doubtfully ;
he knew he was a new-chum, but didn’t want
other people to know it, so a bright idea came into
his head, and he did what somebody once told
him was the right thing to do when purchasing a
horse : he ran his hand down the tendons of the
steed’s fore-leg, and—whistled softly. The little
man went jumping-mad immediately. “Here’s
the finest (fashionable oath) horse in the (fashion-
able) universe, and a (fashionable) new chum
wants to make out he’s screwed.” The new-chum
was staggered at the outburst; still, he felt
he must carry his instructions through to
the end, so he said, “111 give you pounds
for him,” naming half the sum asked. “You’ll
give me blank pounds for him, will you ? Ft r a
horse like that ! Well, dammee if I don’t take it,
just to show you what a fashionable fool you are.
When you’ve had him four-and-twenty hours
you’ll come after me begging me to take the
balance; and I won’t take it; there now.”
They went into the bar, and the little red-bearded
man wrote out a receipt. The new-ohum still
followed out his instructions by asking him to
have a drink, to which the little horse-dealer
assented. “Now,” he said, as he prepared to
start, “ You’re no fashionable new-chum ; you’ve
got that horse at his true value, what did you put
on side for?” “My dear sir,” returned the other,
“I bought him because he had such a nice tail.”
“ Sold again,” said the little man.


A BIG BIZ!
Our Trade is Big! Our Store is Big I
Our Ideas are Big!
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger tlian on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow ?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us! All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear by Us!
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s.real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being ganged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.
If living IN THE COUNTRY, send for our
Catalogue and Patterns of anything you require ;
if in town, call every day.

Ed. Bulletin, —Re tight places. In the
summer of ’BB I was with old Lippy Cue and his
blackboy, travelling vyth a mob of cattle from
Bulloomeyabber, on the Bandicoot, to Borandoran
on the Jogalight. Lying under a tree one very
hot day I fell asleep, with my arms spread out; I
awoke with a feeling of suffocation, and to my
horror found that a huge carpet-snake had swal-
lowed me feet-foreraost right up to the armpits,
and, being unable to get me any further down his
throat, the brute was making off with me as fast
as he could. I roared like a bull, which brought
old Lippy to my assistance. With an axe he
managed to cut off about 16ft. of the reptile ; then
with his sheath-knife he liberated me from my
prison. The snake measured 34ft. 10|in. —W.
W. L., Oloncurry, Q.
A beautiful mixture of religion and biz. is
a pamphlet just to hand, issued by a firm of
optical-instrument makers, and entitled “ The
Gospel Lantern Mission, the greatest soul-winning
work of the age.” “No power was ever so great ”
(as our lantern) “to draw people to Christ. We
have had no quarrels in our church since we bought
our lantern.” “ The street singing at your lantern
service has won me to Christ.” “ Our minister is
sharp—he saw the advantages of lantern work.
We took .£7O in one evening. It is a great help
in the good work.” Alas, the day is coming when
church walls will advertise variety shows ; when
the text will read “Clothe the Naked —with our
50s. suits when the preacher will denounce (and
advertise) M'Glue whisky: “ Beware of M‘Glue,
my friends, it is tempting; but it is a fiery demon.
It is far cheaper than other brands, and, there-
fore, the more are ye likely to be led astray.
Oh, beware of M‘Glue, beloved brethren.” Then
the Bibles and hymn-books will be interleaved in
re the New Liver Pill, and “ Give us this day our
daily bread,” will be used to glorify Jones’ butter
from the Gong Gong Creamery.

In a Barber’s Chair .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The curses of the barber’s life, sir,” said the
operator, as he held me fast by the nose, “are
lads, chestnuts, and parsons. Every man has a
lad and every man saves it to show it off upon
las barber. One customer won’t have our brushes
used upon las hair—fancies they are used anyhow,
like a shoeblack s. Another customer, who dyes
las hair, won’t use our dye—he says he makes his
own, at a cost of sixpence a gallon. That man
came in one morning with the skin off one side of
his face, where the home-made dye had run down.
About one out of a dozen customers growing grey
get hair-dye from us. It’s not all mere vanity, for
a man often loses his billet if he gets grey now-a-
days.
About 19 out of 20 fight against baldness, by
buying hair washes, and so on, from us. flair-
washes ! No good at all, sir—might as well try to
make mulga-bushes grow on their heads. Then
we have one gentleman who won’t look in the
glass he s the only man like that I ever came
across in 20 years’ experience. Most of our
customers enjoy looking in the glass-nearly all
of em keep their chins so low down, on purpose to
see themselves, that we find it very hard to shave
them. But this gentleman, sir, who hates the
glass, has his chair reversed, so that he shall face
the wall. He s a bit mad. A doctor who comes
here regularly tells us he’s only eccentric; but
he’ll go there all right in time.
“No two men have their hair cut alike, sir.
One wants it long over his ears, and another has
it shaved there ; another has it shaved at the back,
and keeps a long top-knot in front to brush up
like a rooster’s ; another has it clipped to the skull
on top, but wants plenty of nap on the back so as
to keep his bump of amativeness warm. Perhaps
one out of four men sits down and lets the barber
cut his hair without comment or interference.
But then, sir, even, he’ll have his fad in shaving
or beard-trimming, and we do 12 shaves for one
hair cut.
“Three men out of ten won’t be shaved against
the grain. One customer always brings his own
towel, and fixes it himself about his neck. Strange
to say, he doesn’t keep a shaving-pot and brush
here; but then only one regular customer out of
10 does. Another regular won’t have water used
upon his face after shaving ; he just wipes the
soap off with a dry towel, pours some oil on his
hair, brushes it himself, pays, and goes. Oil, sir 1
we don’t use much in good shops—a four-ounce
bottle lasts me a fortnight; in a cheap shop you’d
use forty ounces in the time.
“We always wash the shaving-pot and brush
for each new customer, but one customer’s fad is
not to let us do that. While I am brushing the
last customer s coat, he slips into the chair and
lathers himself with the brush, soap, and water
that have just been used on the last man. It’s not
that he’s in a hurry, sir, like most of our cus-
tomers, who are business men ; on the contrary,
he’ll stop an hour but what he’ll have every sprig
cleaned off. It’s his fad, sir ! just his fad. About
one-third of our customers sit down in the chair
with an air of ‘ I’m in for a comfortable loaf.’
“Most men prefer bay-rum to powder after
shaving, but more men than not will have powder
used as well as bay-rum. And, with regard to
fads, I can say that no man knows what a fad is
until he’s gone in for beard-trimming. We give
our customers a hand-glass to go into details with,
while Hie big mirror in front shows them the whole
picture. It s ‘ Here ! here ! here ! There ! there !
there ! Oh ! oh ! steady ! oh ! Don’t ! Damn
it!’ It’s a trying job, sir, when a cronk-faced
man with a cronk beard swears at you because
you can t make him full-faced and handsome.
“Chestnuts! We hear every new story 100
sir, and not one man in 100 can tell it.
And gossip, sir ! If you knew the gossip the
barber hears it would raise your hair. I know
your profession, sir, your favorite restaurant and
favorite dish. See the gentleman behind, sir? A
dozen of my customers have told me about him.
I know how many sisters he has, and brothers;
what disease his father died of ; and what woman
he’s sweet on. The barber’s shop is the school for
scandal, and by degrees we get a true or false
account of every one of our customers from one
another. It’s this way, sir. I’ll be shaving a
gentleman and he’ll say to me, ‘ See the johnny in
the third chair from here?’ ‘Yes, sir,’ I’ll say.
‘ He gets -£l5O a year and does the grand at races,
theatres, billiards, &c. ; d dif 1 know how he
does it; I get twice as much as he does, but I
can’t go his pace.’ Then we are given the
spicy bits of evidence that the court hears with
closed doors ; we can only rely on getting that in
this shop, sir. We hear what barrister or solicitor
is mashed on an actress or music-hall beauty. Our
customers, too, know all about sport, horse-racing,
cricket, football, and the rest; they lay down the
law, and foretell sure winners with great
authority. We get tips from customers for every
sporting event. Oh, yes, we had enough of the
first test-match; even the few priests who
patronise us talked about that.
“Priests behave very differently from parsons
in the barber’s chair. Priests never mention
religious matters at all; they converse freely
enough about such ordinary topics as a priest can
speak on with propriety talk cricket, for
instance. But parsons, sir ! we have a prize one
coming here. He comes here to get his hair cut
and his beard trimmed, because his wife has a
baby and can’t do it now. Nobody can cut hair
but his missus. ‘ Ah,’ he says, ‘ you can’t do it as
nicely as my wife.’ And all' the time I’m clipping
at him, sir, it’s God ! God ! God ! and God is
a name which, when canted about in that
style, the world has grown tired of. You
mustn’t notice my religious opinions, sir, for I
haven’t any ; my religion is that when God made
the world the Devil laughed.”
That’s it 1 -; verbatim, as far as I can remember ;
and all for sixpence, with a shave thrown in !
Adelaide.
G.G.
A TRAGEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In horror mute that chained me to the place,
I watched the awful working of her face,
Distorted as if writhing in some pain
Deep-rooted, which she strove to ease in vain.
Alas, had some dread tram-car vilely blurr’d
The promise of a bright young life?—absurd !
Beside the “ Fresh Food ” window o’er the street,
A girl was eating tarts—and indiscreet.
THE BARD.
Chow clients of the Sydney Chinese
journal are cute. Many, when ordering the paper,
write in Chinese, and put a Id. stamp on the en-
velope, leaving the sides open. As the P.O.
officials cannot read,the characters, the letters go
through.
An explanation to the Whys and Hows i
The rich are fed upon the milk of cows.
well might wonder if the cow should laugh,
But not that Johnnie has become a calf.
There are many exciting episodes in the
life of a Queensland pressman. One of the literary
brotherhood who earned his humble crust in a
coast-town lately made his usual evening call at
the police-station to inquire for news. He was an
amateur wrestler, and so was the officer on duty,
so, after having a drink and swapping lies, they
started a friendly rough-and-tumble. Just when
they were both rolling on the floor, the inspector
stalked in. “ Who’s this ?” he demanded sourly.
“A ferocious and devastating dhrunk, sorr,” said
the startled policeman : “ I’m just securing him,
sorr.” The inspector promptly got on top of the
pressman, and between them he was “secured”
and thrown into a cell. By-and-bye, when the
coast was clear, the policeman went to the cell
and found his friend purple with indignation and
trying to kick the door down. “ I’m sorry to
have had to arrest yez,” he said apologetically,
“ but now that yer in and I’m on juty ye’ve got to
stop that noise.” Then there was a sudden assault,
and the row brought the inspector on the scene a
second time. “ What’s this row ?” he demanded
tersely. “ It’s the same prisoner, sorr,” said
the officer, “securing” his captive a second
time, “he’s gone ravin’ mad.” Then there was
peace for a few minutes more till the coast was
clear, when the officer turned up stealthily with a
bottle of beer and suggestions for a treaty. “If
ye’ll keep quiet,” he said, solemnly, “I’ll put
down yer name as Smith, an’ I’ll get ye bailed
out, an’ I’ll get someone to answer yer name in the
mornin’ and I’ll pay the fine.” Then they drank
thebeer together, and next morning a shock-headed
person of no account answered to the name of
Smith by previous arrangement, and was fined the
usual 10s.
Editor Bulletin. Some day, one of
the finest steamboat accidents on record will
take place on the N.Q. coast. North Queens-
land is the place where everything that is
condemned and accursed goes, where the
glaring sun looks down on poverty and ill-luck
and disaster, on worked-out goldfields, Chinamen,
niggers, flies, misery, dead-and-gone boom towns,
silted-up harbors, snakes, cockroaches, curses,
decaying populations, premature old age, blight,
hopeless mortgages, and all ma.mer of evil things.
All the old steamers which have been forgotten
on southern shores go up there and plod deso-
lately about its forlorn coast. All the old captains
who have fallen into disrepute drift up there also.
Half the time the old steamer is mostly manned
by a depressed variety of beach-comber escaping
from the dismal hell of North Queensland. These
individuals, as a rule, never appear on the ship’s
articles, and when one of them some day or other
steers a big boat to utter wreck, the insurance co.
will decline to pay up for the smash wrought by
the destructive amateur. Discipline on board a
cargo-boat prowling around the remoter parts
of the N.Q. coast is a remarkable affair. Gener-
ally the lights are hung out at night, but some-
times they aren’t. Very often, on a dark night,
there is no one in sight on deck save the man at the
wheel; the officer in charge, the watch, and the
look-out are all asleep and invisible. Sometimes
the steersman also takes short naps, hanging over
the wheel, and then till he wakes the vessel
thunders along and takes care of itself. They
don’t mind these little things much in the Far
North —in fact, they don’t mind anything very much
up there. Nobody is more than half alive in tropical
Queensland. Nobody expects anything very
definitely, except to make “tucker” and boots.
Nobody hopes for anything except to get away,
and up beyond Cooktown it is an understood
thing now that it takes eight years to accumulate
the passage-money to Sydney. When once a man
gets into the Gulf country, unless he is content
to hang his only pair of boots over his
shoulders, and march straight southward along
the sand-track, and never stop till he gets to
Sydney, he can count on spending the best years
of his life there, saving up the money to get away
with. Half the prospectors who invade the Far
North never get away at all; the fatalism of the
accursed land gets hold of them, and they either
flicker away the rest of their lives in pottering
around after elusive gold, or else go and live with
the blacks. The eternal rains and shadows of the
scrub country bleach them a sickly white, like
Gehazi the leper, and they run wild like the local
cassowary; and the old men who have, outlined
their other delusions mostly have a vague idea
that some day they will get away to their heaven,
which is Cooktown, and that someone there will
trust them with a week’s board and lodging, and
that they will get drunk. But very few of them
do. And a country where a man’s highest ambi-
tion is to get to Cooktown is a pitiful place,
anyhow.—T.S.
Dear Bulletin, —Do newspapers take
civil servants’ screed because it is cheap ? I
doubt it. “ Spagnoletti ”is not consistent. He
argues on the cheap theory, and at the same time
quotes a subdised pressman’s earnings at <£3 a
week. No civil servants' stuff is cheap at that
price. Many State employes contribute articles
of special character, and are paid specially high
rates. They, being specialists, really compete
with no one. Finally, I know two instances in
which civil servants ceased contributing as soon
as their bills were cut down below current rates. —
Cop.
COOL—FOR BOURKE.
“ The weather was what is called cool for Bourke."-
Sydney D.T. travelling reporter.
What? The weather? Well, the weather,
If you take it altogether,
Was a thing a salamander wouldn’t shirk,
There are hotter times in Sheol
For a white man or a Creole ;
It was what you might call middling cool—for
Bourke !
P’r’aps you know the sort the plight is
When you’re stewing-up pyrites
With the choking fumes of sulphur all at work ;
Well, the weather was like that is,
Mixing dust where melting fat is.
So you know that it was middling cool —for
Bourke!
The thermometers recorded
That the cool the shade afforded
Was a trifle o’er a hundred and a jerk ;
Which was icily amazing
With one-fifty “in the blazing”—
On the whole, you see, ’twas middling cool—for
Bourke!
We could grill our chops by sunlight
(That is, those who like ’em done light),
But the dust it made a curtain, like, of murk;
And we had to, willy nilly,
Use a lens to “ boil the billy,”
So you’ll see that it was really cool—for Bourke!

The Monkey or the Man ?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He was the outcome of generations of
crime; a degrader of women, a thief, liar, and
betrayer of “ pals a thing to which nothing was
sacred and the meaning of “friend” unknown.
Eor one of many murders he was condemned and
hung.
In his interview with the Devil, he preserved a
jaunty and defiant air, as though to say : “Now
you’ve got me—well ?”
The Devil smiled —his own peculiar twister.
“ Send you to hell ?” he said. “ Yes, I’m going
to, but not my hell —it’s not hot enough. Man
you shall be again—yet not man. Wfeu —ugh !”
And the whiff of fiery breath forced the man
upwards to earth, into the wilds of a forest in
Borneo.
* * *
The maternal orang-outang had reared
many young ones, but her seventh surpassed them
all in cunning and devilry. He speedily became
the terror of the ape-world, but to its joy he was
early captured and sold to the manager of a great
circus.
This man could make nothing of his purchase ;
for months the creature sulked and starved, until,
repenting of his bargain, the manager contem-
plated selling it again.
Then came the new lady rider, really pretty
under her paint, vivacious, young and charming.
“ Poor creature, ” she said in genuine pity, “it
looks so miserable and cold. See, it shivers !”
“Pure cussedness,” laughed the gentleman
rider, who stood beside her ; “ trust a monkey to
trick you if he can. ”
“ It’s not only the monkeys can do that, she
retorted. The man drew back with an angry
flush ; the ape grinned. .
“It’s positively laughing,” she cried. ‘I like
it, I do indeed. I’ll adopt and train it,’’unfasten-
ing the cage as she spoke.
“ You’re mad !” shouted the man, striving to
close the door. In the scuffle the ape escaped.
Instead of using his freedom the brute clutched
Miss Madeline’s dress, and cowered beside her,
his little, deep-set eyes twinkling up at hers,
violet and soft with pity.
The man strode towards them; the ape showed
his fangs in silent menace.
“Don’t touch him,” she ordered, dropping her
soft fingers upon the bristly head. “Kindness is
the only cure for him.”
After that the orang-outang went by the name
of “ Miss Madeline’s Man,” and the rider who
coveted that title ground his teeth in futile ex-
pression of the jealous hatred he bore the dumb
brute.
The fame of the girls power over the ape
4 vmght crowds to the show nightly, and marvel-
lous feats were performed by the two.
Even the riding with the man was a small affair
in comparison ; and it was noticeable that during
this the ape tugged' at his chain in frantic
endeavour to be free, and watched her every move-
ment with all but human interest.
Then, in turn, the man would stand with folded
arms and lowering brow while the girl and the ape
iterformed ; at each touch of the hairy hands upon
her person loathing and resentment showed in
every line of his face.
The man was a clever rider, accustomed to leap
from the ground to the bare back of a galloping
horse and maintain his balance without using his
On one occasion the ape squatted on his haunches
beside the ringj unchained, in readiness for his own
performance with Miss Madeline.
As the man made his rush to mount the gallop-
ing horse, the ape screamed shrilly, arresting his
attention and baulking the leap. The crowd jeered,
Again the man attempted the feat, landing suc-
cessfully upon the bare rump, but slipping back
immediately. The delighted spectators hooted
and yelled,
The ape joined his derisive cries to theirs, and
the infuriated man, bailing up the blameless
horse, cut it smartly over the face with his riding-
whip. Then the frightened beast fairly flew round
the ring, and, with the increased speed, the man
found less difficulty in remaining upright and
triumphant upon its back after his next leap,
The crowd cheered now, but the ape, grim and
silent, crouched against the flapping calico, and,
as the horse came past him, sprang upon its
haunches and seized the man by the ankles,
dragging him with terrible force to the ground,
where he lay stunned and unconscious,
The knockabouts rushed upon the ape with
picks and mauls, but before they could harm him
Miss Madeline, in tights and spangles, had thrown
herself between her pet and his danger. With an
arm around his thick neck she led him away. Ihe
crowd cheered anew—their sympathy was with
the monkey, not the man.
After that the rider, loving the girl desperately,
kept his distance from the ape, watching his
opportunity for vengeance. She, knowing this,
kept the brute beside her always, as much to
tantalise the man as to protect the ape.
Meantime the show travelled far.
r U P° n one still moonlight night, after the per-
formance, when the heat had driven everyone out
of doors rather than into stuffy bedrooms, Miss
Madeline and the rider strolled to the outskirts of
the village in which the tents were pitched,
Though she would not marry him, she liked to
draw him on, to incite him to fresh pleading, to
hold him still her own. .
They had forgotten the ape, which had sneaked
out behind them and noiselessly followed within
hearing, creeping in the shadows out of sight,
Presently they leaned upon a fence-rail and
looked out over the moonlit country. She turned
her head at the touch of his hand upon hers,
“I* do that again I shall go back she
said. As I have often told you, you may look,
but you mustn’t touch.” _
He was in no mood for jests.
“Yet you let that brown devil paw you,” ho
retorted rudely.
‘ It would be well if you were to take a lesson
in manners from that brown gentleman, she
mocked.
The ape came closer, his gimlet eyes dancing as
though he understood her words.
“ I believe you love the brute he said bitterly.
“ Oh, no, I love the monkey, I have no feeling
for the brute ! ” she sneered, with pointed
reference.
An evil look rose in his eyes,
The ape, watching him, pressed close against her
dress on the opposite side ; accustomed to his pre-
sence, she barely noticed his hand clasp hers,
The rider spoke again m low-toned menace,
“ Some day you will taunt me beyond endurance
I s a V m j^ r even . . , ~
Jealous ! > Of a monkey ! And you call your-
self a man ?
“ Yes ! but, by God, you shall never tell it,” he
cried, hoarsely, and, seizing her throat with the
fearful strength of fury, he forced her backwards
against the rail.
The sudden twist dislocated her neck; her
expiation was swift and merciful.
As her limp body slid to the earth, the ape
peered closely into her face, his yellow eyes intense
with fear, while he uttered inarticulate sounds
which ill expressed his anguish. Then he lifted
her lifeless hand, which, for the first time, did not
respond to his touch.
He understood!
The man, looking down upon his work, groaned
in the agony of returning v reason. The ape rose
upon his hind-legs and faced him.
The man recoiled from the human pain and
hatred burning in the eyes which held his own,
and lost his chance. In a second the ape’s hands
closed round his throat.
With all his might the man sent his fist bang
into the ape’s wind. The creature gasped and
choked, loosening its grasp a little so that the man
drew breath, but it still hung on.
The man drew and opened his pocket-knife, and
jammed it deep into the hairy breast. The beast
bared its fangs and grunted as a gush of blood fol-
lowed the stab, but the muscular fingers tightened
on the man’s throat until his protruding eyes,
suffused with blood, saw but the indistinct outline
of the ape, which swayed before him.
A moment more, and they fell together across
the huddled form of the woman they had both
loved.
* * *
The man was her murderer, but which
was her womanhood’s martyred avenger the
monkey, or the man ?
Maoriland.
MABEL HOLMES.
No Jew has ever been brought before the

Dear Bulletin, —Ben. Smith (bullocky)
took 13 tons 15ewt. wool from Mahonga (Eland’s)
Station to Wahgunyah (Vic.), about 10 years ago,
in one load. There were 100 bales, some scoured,
and Smith claimed this to be the champion load.
He had 18 bullocks in the team. —Yours truly,
Avendale.

The Furices.
(With Apologies to the W.C.T.U.)
AFTER LESSING.
“ Old and weary are my Furies,” murmured Pluto,
lord of hell;
“Old and useless are they, Hermes ; had I new
ones it were well.
Seek, thou messenger of Hades, Upper Earth, and
bring me three
Of her daughters, stern, unbending, and from
doting weakness free.”
Spake in Heaven to Iris Juno: “Iris, seek the
Lower Earth ;
Go and hither bring me maidens, cold and chaste,
of mortal birth;
Bring them hither, gentle Iris, for I fain would
laugh to scorn
Venus boasting hers the Empire over ev’ry woman
born ! ”
Wafted down by gentle zephyrs on her many-
colored wing.
Iris floated, and her coming brought along
delicious spring ;
And she sought and sought the maidens, and she
sought and sought again
Till with saddened heart she turned her —for her
seeking was in vain.
And when Juno saw her fav’rite thus returning
all alone,
Loud her queenly voice she lifted, “ Hath the
Earth so worthless grown ?
Is the virtue all departed from the lands beneatli
the sun
That, of maidens cold and spotless, thou canst
bring me never one I"
“ Goddess,” answered Iris gently, “ true it is that
Maidens Three
On the Lower Earth existed, from the bonds of
Venus free ;
Maidens in whose breasts unmaidenly there burned
no tender flame,
Maidens who had never smiled on man—alas ! too
late I came !”
“Late?” cried Juno, all astounded. “Ay! for
Hermes came before,
And to Pluto’s gates infernal straight the icy trio
bore.”
“ What doth Pluto with the maidens ?” Answered
Iris, “Goddess, know
He hath taken them for Furies to his gloomy realms
below.”
